==PAGE:3==
3 
 
Abstract 
A mechanistic code has been developed to simulate the non-standard loading conditions 
excreted by heavy off-road vehicles, with tracks or flotation tires. The non-standard contact 
area between the vehicles and the pavement are approximated by multiple non-overlapping 
micro circles. By doing this the calculations can be based on layered elastic theory with circular 
loads. The rolling motion of tracks and flotation tires is modelled by sequentially lowering and 
lifting a loading area from the pavement surface. The code was validated against field 
experiments conducted with a dump truck, an IFV, and a semi-truck. The effect of 
implementing a reinforcement grid to the asphalt concrete layer, as an input for the code was 
investigated. It was found that the best way for reducing strains in the bottom of the AC was to 
place the grid at the bottom. The grid was found to do little and no difference for the vertical 
stresses at the top of the base layer and subgrade respectively. By utilizing the code, the peaks 
of five key responses were examined on four different synthetic pavement systems, and at two 
different speeds. The key responses were compared with the responses from a standard axle. It 
was found that the dump truck resulted in higher tensile strains along with higher compressive 
and shear stresses, than a standard axle, everywhere considered, except at the top of the 
subgrade and at the top of the subbase for the stronger pavement systems considered. It was 
also found that the IFV resulted in higher compressive and shear stresses, than a standard axle, 
everywhere considered, except at the top of the subbase, where the stresses were lower for 
some of the pavement systems. The strains at the bottom of the AC depended a lot on the 
pavement system, with stronger systems resulting in lower strains, when compared to the 
strains caused by a standard axle.

1 Introduction 
1.1 Background and motivation 
Asphalt 
pavements 
are 
the 
most 
common 
pavement 
type 
worldwide 
(pavements.pitt.edu/Wiki/Wiki.aspx accessed 17.01.2023). This type of transportation 
infrastructure is composed of engineered soils compacted in layers and overlaid with asphalt 
concrete. In addition to soil-like materials and asphalt concrete, pavements are sometimes 
reinforced with geo-synthetics (Laurinavičius 2006); these products are designed to prolong 
the service life of new/rehabilitated pavements, i.e., extend the duration in-between 
rehabilitation activities.  
Pavement systems are mainly designed to serve loads exerted by vehicles equipped with road 
tires. However, in certain circumstances, such infrastructure must serve vehicles intended for 
off-road operations such as tanks, dump trucks, and agricultural equipment. These vehicles are 
relatively heavy machines, commonly weighing hundreds of kilonewtons and traveling at slow 
speeds. They are almost exclusively equipped with tracks (either rubber or metallic) or flotation 
tires (or both). This study is motivated by the concern that when such off-road vehicles are 
allowed on asphalt roads, the remaining service life may be shortened, given the non-standard 
loading conditions. 
The analysis of asphalt pavements for new design and rehabilitation design is currently based 
on layered elastic theory (e.g., Vejregler 2022). This mechanistic framework assumes that 
pavement systems can be modelled as stratified half-spaces made of elastic layers; it provides 
responses (i.e., stresses, strains, and displacements) everywhere in the medium due to vertical 
loads applied at the surface, uniformly distributed over circular areas. This framework has been 
validated to adequately reproduce and forecast responses in full-scale constructions. The 
validation efforts were based on comparing calculated responses to corresponding responses 
measured by buried instrumentation. In general terms, such a modelling and validation 
approach will be utilized herein to investigate the effects of off-road vehicles on both 
unreinforced and reinforced systems.  
1.2 Structure of the report 
The report starts with a literature review covering existing research dealing with: (i) off-road 
vehicles, and their expected effects, either measured or modelled, on asphalt pavements, (ii) 
methods for field measurement of pavement responses, (iii) layered elastic theory and its 
implementation in code, and (iv) asphalt reinforcement, field observations and modelling 
attempts.  Following the literature review the objective and methodology of the study are 
presented. This is followed by a description of the field work, including the preliminary tests, 
test setup, main experiment, and results. Afterwards, the mechanistic code is explained, 
verified, and validated. The mechanistic code is then used on synthetic pavements to compare 
the pavement responses from the vehicles used in the main experiment. The study terminates 
with a summary of the main findings, conclusions, and recommendations for future work. 
 
 
==PAGE:7==
7 
 
2 Literature review 
2.1 Effects of off-road vehicles on asphalt pavements 
In a study by Sebaaly et al. (2003), the objective was to evaluate the effect of agriculture 
equipment on flexible pavements. The evaluations were done based on experiments performed 
on different agriculture off-road vehicles equipped with flotation tires. The vehicles were a 
Grain cart 875-16 pulled by a tractor, a Terra-gator model 8103, a Terra-gator model 8144 and 
a rubber tracked tractor. These vehicles are then compared to a standard truck with a single 
axle load of 18,000lb (~8165 kg). The test is done on two different asphalt pavement roads 
with different subgrades and with two different asphalt layer thicknesses per road. The test was 
conducted in different seasons, fall, spring, and summer. Resulting in different temperatures 
for the asphalt respectively 95°F (35°C), 41°F (5°C) and 106°F (41°C). Strain ratios were 
calculated between the farming vehicles and the truck, by dividing the farming vehicle 
pavement response with the truck pavement response. 
From the measurements the following conclusions were made: The empty farming vehicles 
were equal to or less damaging than the truck. The farming vehicles loaded were more 
damaging than the truck in the three tested seasons. It was also found that there were higher 
strain ratios on one of the asphalt roads, since it was constructed on top of an older asphalt 
pavement resulting in the pavement having better bonding and bending strength. Another 
reason was that the strain on the other road was higher for the truck which resulted in lower 
strain ratios for the road. 
 
The Danish Road Directorate (2020) has explored heavy off-road vehicles with tracks and 
flotation tires used for agriculture. The objective of the work was to research the attrition effects 
of the heavy off-road vehicles compared to “legal” alternatives. The reason for the research of 
these types of vehicles is they must be transported to and from the field. The research can be 
used to investigate if the rules need changes, to potentially allow for heavy off-road vehicles 
to drive on the road. The vehicles testes with were a Grimme Maxtron (beat harvester), a 
Ploeger EPD540 (pea harvester), an oversize-trailer/semi-truck vehicle loaded with the 
Grimme Maxtron, a semi-truck with tanker trailer 7-axle 56t, and a tractor with slurry trailer. 
The experiments are focusing on the impact a vehicle has on the road and consists of a fatigue 
test, the damages on the surface, pressure measurements in depth in the road, axle load test, 
and contact pressure test. The results of the tests show that the road with surface dressing, gets 
indents and bleeding from the tracks, after 200 runs. For hot mix asphalt roads there were no 
indents and bleeding. Since bleeding happened, the test was stopped after 200 runs and 
therefore it is unknown if rutting would have happened. For a test with the Grimme Maxtron 
on the oversize-trailer, rutting appeared. A tight turning test for the Grimme Maxtron and 
oversize-trailer semi with the Grimme Maxtron on did not result in any surface damage on a 
hot mix asphalt road. The pressure in depth measurements showed that the off-road vehicles 
did not have higher pressure than other vehicles. It was also found there was higher pressure 
when the Grimme Matron was on the trailer than if it was driven by itself. The contact pressure 
and axle load test showed that the tracks did not result in higher pressure than the tires. The 
oversize-trailer semi with Grimme Maxtron and the semi-truck with tanker-trailer resulted in 
the highest contact pressure. It was also found that it was the ribs under the rollers that were 
passing the load to the surface (i.e. low to non-existent load from the track between the rollers). 
==PAGE:8==
8 
 
 
In a recent work by Hesthaven (2021), the objective was threefold: (i) experimentally 
investigating and documenting the effects of tracked vehicles, (ii) comparing the responses of 
the vehicles with a standard axle’s responses, and (iii) developing a mechanistic-based code 
that models the pavement response from tracked vehicles. 
The work included investigating the pavement responses for three different agriculture vehicles 
and a semi-truck and comparing them to modelled responses from a mechanistic code. The 
agriculture vehicles consisted of a Grimme Maxtron 620 II (a beat harvester with tracks and 
flotation tires), a Ploeger EPD540 (a pea harvester with tracks and flotation tires), and a Fendt 
MT1100 (a tractor with tracks). The measured pavement response was the displacement in the 
transverse direction, which was measured alongside the pitch, and the roll of the pavement 
surface. The geometry of the tracks and tires, and the weight distributions of the vehicles were 
also measured and used in the modelling of the responses. The pavement properties were found 
by performing DCP and FWD tests.  
The mechanistic code developed in the study is capable of calculating the pavement responses 
from tracked vehicles. The code was built on top of the LET module of the open-source code 
ALVA (Adaptive Layered Viscoelastic Analysis) made by Skar et al. (2020a). An explanation 
of how ALVA works was given in Skar et al. (2020b). The LET module in ALVA is based on 
Burmister (1945) a multi-layered elastic theory. By basing it on Burmister it is assumed that 
all the layers are homogeneous linear elastic, isotropic and weightless. Other assumptions taken 
in the code are that vehicles traverse the pavement at crawling speed so inertia effects can be 
neglected, the moving speed is constant, surface loads are modelled quasi-statically and 
viscoelastic effects are not included, and surface loading is exerted on a smooth, linear, and 
non-inclined pavement. The imprint from the tracked vehicles were modelled as rectangular 
patches that were approximated by circles. The circles all had the same radius and were 
arranged to fill up the patch as much as possible without overlapping. Superposition was used 
on the responses of the individual circles to get the total response. The precision of the 
approximation was examined by filling a rectangular patch with increasingly small circles and 
comparing the vertical displacement with the results from closed form half space solutions. 
The examination showed that the error with the approximation was decreasing with an increase 
in the number of circles. The code was also verified against the program ELLEA1 (Levenberg 
2016). In this verification strains and displacements in two systems, namely a half-space and a 
three-layered system, were compared. With an appropriate number of circles, the errors were 
found to be minimal. The pavement responses from the vehicles were used to validate the code.  
The mechanistic code was used to carry out an analysis on three synthetic pavement systems 
consisting of 3 layers (asphalt concrete, base, and subgrade). The three systems were chosen to 
imitate different Danish countryside road types, divided into a strong, medium, and weak 
system. The responses from the vehicles used in the experiment were compared to a standard 
axle, namely an Equivalent Single Axle Load’s (ESAL) responses. The ESAL being a dual 
axle with a load of 10 ton, used as a standard load for road design (e.g., in Vejregler 2022). For 
all three systems the semi-truck and ESAL resulted in larger responses, for the maximum 
tensile strain at the top and bottom of the asphalt layer, for the maximum vertical stress at the 
top of the base layer, and for the maximum octahedral shear stress in the asphalt layer. For the 
maximum vertical stress at the top of the subgrade the Grimme Maxtron 620 II had the largest 
response for the strong and medium systems, while the semi-truck and ESAL had the largest 
==PAGE:9==
9 
 
responses for the weak system. For the medium system the semi-truck and ESAL had larger 
responses than the Ploeger EPD540 and Fendt MT1100. For the strong system the ESAL had 
the smallest response followed by the Ploeger EPD540. The semi-truck had a smaller response 
than the Fendt MT1100.  
 
2.2 Method for field measurement of pavement responses 
In broad terms, the measurement of pavement responses is done by sensors embedded in the 
structure and subgrade (i.e., in-pavement sensing) or by a movable platform containing an array 
of sensors. In both cases, the sensors record the effects of a known load applied at the surface. 
In Rabe (2018) the general objective of the paper is to give a short overview of pavement 
instrumentation and sensor design, give examples and evaluation of response measurements. 
The general reason for pavement sensing is gaining information about the pavement behaviour 
for different loadings and boundary conditions. This can then be used for modelling the 
pavement and validating already created models. The sensing can also be used to gain 
information about the performance of the pavement, if it loses stiffness and adhesion in the 
interface and so on. It can also be used for assisting in back calculation for finding the pavement 
system’s moduli and be used to correlate measurements with deflections found by for example 
Falling Weight Deflectometer (FWD) tests to get an improved interpretation of the deflections, 
among other things. The FWD test measures the surface deflections. Other sensors such as 
strain gauges, pressure cells, and accelerometers can also provide response results from the 
pavement that can be used to predict the pavements performance and or remaining lifetime. 
These sensors can be embedded in and on the pavement. The FWD is not embedded but 
measured from a movable platform. All the different methods lead to the overall same 
conclusion, a measurement of the pavement response, which could be displacements, strains 
and or stresses. 
When considering in-pavement sensing, the types of pavement systems and sensors need to be 
thought through, since the sensors discontinue the material structure and can disturb the 
material stiffness, which can be minimized with the right sensors. The sensors need to be a 
certain size compared to the grains in the pavement for good results from the sensors. 
A sensor such as a strain gauge can not mimic the varying moduli of asphalt and therefore a 
sensors modulus needs to be low enough to be useful. A recommendation is a modulus of 500 
MPa, for asphalt at 40 °C, or a lower modulus. The sensor also needs to withstand the heat and 
impacts when paving. 
From Barriera et al. (2020) the objective was to give a detailed review on instrumentation for 
pavements, that can aid in development of an operational in situ monitoring system for road 
pavements.  Strain measurements can be very useful for analysing the pavement response under 
moving loads. They can also be very useful for predicting the reduction in bearing capacity of 
the pavement, because at the bottom of the asphalt there can be a high amount of strain after 
repeated loading, which can lead to fatigue failure. A sensor type for this can be a strain gauge. 
The strain in the pavement is also exerted on the embedded strain gauge and thereby a 
measurement of the pavement response is obtained. Other sensors that can be used embedded, 
are sensors such as fiber optic sensors (FOS), linear variable differential transformers (LVDT), 
accelerometers, geophones, pressure cells and temperature sensors. 
==PAGE:10==
10 
 
The FOS is based on optical fiber connected to a light source to measure certain elements, such 
as strain. It is a very useful sensor type because of the small footprint and high precision. The 
LVDT can be used to measure the vertical deflection in the pavement. This is done by 
measuring the change in voltage from the reference rod placed, so the surface loading does not 
interfere, and this type of sensor can also be very precise. Geophones and accelerometers have 
a similar measurement of vertical deflection. The accelerometer measures velocity changes, 
that can be integrated twice to get the deflection. The geophone measures the displacement 
velocity from a moving load and by integrating it, the deflection can be obtained.  Pressure 
cells measure the compressive stress from the load. Overall, there are two different types, a 
hydraulic and diaphragm pressure cell. 
The in-pavement embedded sensors have some advantages compared to the other methods, 
such as FWD. The embedded sensors can be used to continuously monitor the pavement, and 
they do not obstruct the traffic. These sensors can then be used to create “smart roads”.  
 
2.3 Layered elastic theory and its implementation in code 
The theory of layered elasticity was initiated by Burmister (1943, 1945) who proposed an 
analytic solution for a stratified half-space composed of linear elastic materials and loaded over 
a circular area.  Mechanistic modelling can be based on numerical calculations of an analytical 
stress/strain problem. The equations of elasticity are from the general mathematical theory of 
elasticity from A. E. Love and S. Timoshenko. The theory is as follows: 
The equilibrium equations:

Where E is the modulus, 𝜈 is Poisson’s ratio, and 𝜙 is the stress function. z is the vertical 
distance to the evaluation point, and r is the horizontal distance to the evaluation point from 
the middle of the circular load. 
The general solution for the stress equations and displacement equations can be found by the 
stress function which must satisfy the compatibility equations. The following stress function 
satisfies the compatibility equations: 
𝜙(𝑟,  𝑧) = 𝐽0(𝑚𝑟)(𝐴𝑒𝑚𝑧−𝐵𝑒−𝑚𝑧+ 𝐶𝑧𝑒𝑚𝑧−𝐷𝑧𝑒−𝑚𝑧) 
(10) 
z is the depth, r is the distance horizontal to the evaluation point, m is an integration parameter. 
A(m), B(m), C(m), and D(m) are functions dependent on coordinates r and z. 𝐽0 is the zero order 
Bessel function. 
From the general mathematical linear elastic theory Burmister (1943, 1945) established a 
solution for a two-layer system and a three-layer system. This is done by substituting the stress 
function (10) into the equations for stress and displacement (4)-(9). The Burmister solution 
assumes the following conditions: 
• The materials are homogeneous, isotropic, elastic materials for which Hooke’s law is 
valid. 
• The layers are assumed to be weightless and infinite in the horizontal direction 
• No normal and shear stress at the surface outside the load area. At infinite depth the 
stresses and strains are 0. 
• All layers except the bottom layer have a finite thickness, h. The bottom layer is infinite 
in thickness. 
• The surface is uniformly loaded over a circular area with radius a. 
• No body forces 
From the theory Burmister (1945) has formulated two continuity conditions, where analytical 
solutions have been found. Case 1: Continuity between the layers is assumed, resulting in the 
stresses and displacements in the interface being equal for both layers, only the horizontal radial 
stress has a discontinuity across the interface. Case 2: The two layers are continuously in 
contact but with a frictionless interface and a continuity of normal stresses and normal 
displacements only. 
 
==PAGE:12==
12 
 
For a layered elastic mechanistic code such as ALVA (Skar et al. 2020a), the solution is based 
on further development of the analytical solutions from Burmister (1945). This solution works 
for multiple layers. The method ALVA uses to solve this problem is examined in Jaeseung 
(2011) and Khazanovich and Wang (2007). The modelling has the same assumptions as stated 
above and the same input: moduli, Poisson's ratios, layer thicknesses and loading conditions. 
For the construction of stress and displacement equations for more layers, the stress 
function/biharmonic function can be written for each layer, which adhere to what Burmister 
did 
𝜙𝑖(𝑟,  𝑧) = ∫𝐽0(𝑚𝑟)(𝐴𝑖(𝑚) 𝑒𝑚𝑧−𝐵𝑖(𝑚)𝑒−𝑚𝑧+ 𝐶𝑖(𝑚)𝑧𝑒𝑚𝑧−𝐷𝑖(𝑚)𝑧𝑒−𝑚𝑧)
∞
0

resulting in response functions for each layer. The m is an integration parameter, z is the depth, 
r is the distance horizontal to the evaluation point from the middle of the circular load. The 
Ai(m), Bi(m), Ci(m), and Di(m) are functions dependent on coordinates r and z. 𝐽0 is the zero 
order Bessel function. This can then be used with the equations for stress and displacement (4-
9) as Burmister, where the stress functions are for i-layer.  
A way to find the solution as stated in Khazanovich and Wang (2007) is to first apply Hankel 
transform to the boundary conditions. The boundary conditions are then inserted in the 
response equations, which can be solved for the functions A(m), B(m), C(m), and D(m). After 
these are found, an inverse Hankel transform can then be performed on the response functions 
with the found A(m), B(m), C(m), and D(m) functions.  
The inverse Hankel transform is a more demanding step in finding a solution. So, this can be 
done numerically as stated in Khazanovich and Wang (2007). A solution for an i-layered 
system is based on calculating a solution for a layered elastic system as followed:

 𝑑𝑚= ∑𝑤(𝑚𝑠)𝑓𝑖(𝜇𝑖,  𝐸𝑖,  𝑧,  𝑚𝑠)𝐽1(𝑚𝑠𝑎)𝐽𝑘(𝑚𝑠𝑟)
𝑁

+ 𝜁1 (14) 
Where 𝑚𝑠 is Gaussian integration points, 𝑤(𝑚𝑠) is Gaussian weights and 𝜁1 is the error of 
Gausian integration in each subinterval. 
  
==PAGE:13==
13 
 
The implementation of this in ALVA follows the procedure from Khazanovich and Wang 
(2007). 
1. Solving system of equations, from the boundary conditions, for the evaluation of the 
functions Ai(m), Bi(m), Ci(m), and Di(m), i=1,…,n with selected key values of m. 
2. Calculate the values for function 𝑓𝑖(𝜇𝑖,  𝐸𝑖,  𝑧,  𝑚) (where the functions Ai(m), Bi(m), 
Ci(m), and Di(m) are involved) at all Gaussian quadrature points by interpolating for a 
solution of the function with the key values of m. 
3. Calculate the product of the Bessel functions 𝐽1(𝑚𝑠𝑎)𝐽𝑘(𝑚𝑠𝑟) for the Gaussian 
integration points. This should then be multiplied by the function 𝑓𝑖 at the points. 
4. Calculate the integrals (13) by using the Gaussian quadrature rule (14). 
5. Sum all the integrals (13) to find the approximate linear elastic response. 
  
Linear viscoelastic 
For more advanced modelling of the pavement system linear viscoelastic theory for the asphalt 
concrete layer can be considered. This is described in Levenberg (2013) and Levenberg 
(2016b) and the following is from this literature. A linear viscoelastic system can be 
transformed into an equivalent linear elastic problem. The correspondence principle states a 
Laplace transformed viscoelastic response to a given input can be found from the 
corresponding response in a related elastic problem. The geometry needs to be the same as 
Laplace transformed viscoelastic response. And the elastic parameters should be replaced by a 
s-multiplied Laplace transform viscoelastic parameter functions. The input should also be 
replaced by its Laplace transform. The time dependent response is then found by Laplace 
transform inversion of the related elastic solution. 
This linear viscoelastic system model is similar to the principle of the layered linear elastic 
model stated above is the same, expect one or more layers are changed to be viscoelastic, while 
the rest of the layers are kept linear elastic. 
The response of the elastic problem can be written as followed: 
𝑅𝑒(𝑡) = 𝑅𝐻
𝑒(𝑐1
𝑒, 𝑐2
𝑒, … ) ⋅𝐼(𝑡) = 𝑅𝐻
𝑒(𝐸1, … , 𝐸𝑛; 𝜈1, … , 𝜈𝑛; ℎ1, … , ℎ𝑛; 𝑞; 𝑎) ⋅𝐼(𝑡) 
(15) 
Where 𝑅𝑒(𝑡) is the response and 𝑅𝐻
𝑒(⋅) is a function that contains all the elastic calculations. 
Where 𝑐𝑒 is time independent material properties. The properties, as stated for the response, 
are the moduli E, the Poisson’s ratios 𝜈, the layer thicknesses h, and the load parameters q and 
a. It is also dependent on the evaluation coordinate i.e., the distance from the loading to the 
point of interest. I(t) is the input function, such as the step function Heaveside H(t). To find the 
function that contains the calculations for a corresponding viscoelastic problem, the input is set 
to H(t) and using the elastic-viscoelastic correspondence principal, the resulting function has 
time dependent material properties that are Laplace transformed. The material properties are s-
multiplied Laplace transforms that are corresponding to the viscoelastic material properties 
𝑐𝑣𝑒. H(t) is also Laplace transformed to 1/s. 
𝑅𝐻
𝑣𝑒(𝑠) = 𝑅𝐻
𝑒(𝑐1
𝑣𝑒(𝑠),  𝑐2
𝑣𝑒(𝑠), … ) ⋅(1
𝑠) 
(16) 
Where the bar indicates a Laplace transformed time function. 
==PAGE:14==
14 
 
To remove the s from the Laplace transforms, a Carson transform is defined as the s-multiplied 
Laplace transform. After that the Laplace transform inversion can be done. This last step is 
most of the time impossible to do using non-numerical methods. The Laplace inversion is done 
by using the direct method by Schapery. With this method the viscoelastic response can be 
found to be: 
𝑅𝐻
𝑣𝑒(𝑡) ≈𝑅𝐻
𝑒(𝑐1
𝑣𝑒, 𝑐2
𝑒, … ) ≈𝑅𝐻
𝑒(𝐷(𝑡)−1, 𝐸2, … , 𝐸𝑛; 𝜈1, … , 𝜈𝑛; ℎ1, … , ℎ𝑛; 𝑞; 𝑎) 
(17) 
The response can be found from the elastic function 𝑅𝐻
𝑒, with viscoelastic properties for the 
layers that are viscoelastic, which is a quasi-elastic approximation. In (17) the creep 
compliance D(t) is used for the viscoelastic layer instead of E1, while E2 to En is still used for 
the elastic layers. This method can be directly used for a moving load/vehicle along a line. 
 
2.4 Asphalt reinforcement.  
Graziani et al. (2014) investigated the influence of two types of grid reinforcement on the 
stress-strain response. The objective of the study was to analyse the mechanical response from 
real scale loads on grid reinforced asphalt pavements. The types of grid reinforcement used in 
the study was a glass fiber polymer grid (FP), with a mesh size of 20 mm, and a carbon 
fiber/glass fiber grid (CF), with a mesh size of 33 mm. The FP grid was very stiff while the CF 
grid was very flexible and deformable. The grid reinforcements were installed at the interface 
of a double-layered asphalt surfacing. The testing consisted of FWD tests and real-scale truck 
tests, under which the loads were measured with pressure cells and asphalt strain gauges 
installed inside the pavement. A linear elastic theory model (LET) was used to analyse the 
results. It was found that the average peak strains measured in the FWD tests were reduced in 
the section with the CF grid, while there was no reduction found in the section with the FP grid, 
when comparing to the unreinforced section. For the real-scale tests it was found that the strain 
field inside the asphalt concrete (AC) was reduced for the CF grid, while the FP grid led to an 
increase in the measured longitudinal strains, when comparing to the unreinforced 
measurements. In the LET model the AC layers were modelled as two separate layers, with the 
grid reinforcing effect being represented by an increase in the upper AC layer’s modulus. An 
interface slip parameter was calculated for both reinforced sections, where it was found that 
the FP grid results in a higher debonding than the CF grid. The high slip parameter for the FP 
grid was theorized to be the main cause of the overall increase in the measured strain field. 
Xianrong et al. (2020) investigated the effect of geocell-reinforced asphalt pavements on 
longitudinal strain, with the objective of evaluating the reinforcing effectiveness of geocell 
applied in asphalt pavements. A truck passed a reinforced and an unreinforced section with 3 
different speeds and at 3 different axle loads. The geocell-reinforcement used had a mesh size 
of 20 cm, a thickness of 2 mm, and a height of 3 cm and was laid on top of the base layer 
(extended into the bottom layer of the AC). It was found that the longitudinal strain was smaller 
for the reinforced section and that there was a larger reduction in strain at increased loads and 
at slower speeds. A test was also done where the truck was stationary with the 3 different axle 
loads on both sections. This showed the same results as the tests where the truck passed the 
sections. 
The objective of the study by Nielsen et al. (2022) was to give an outline of a mechanistic 
modelling approach describing an asphalt pavement that was repaired by mill-and-overlay 
==PAGE:15==
15 
 
including an interlayer grid reinforcement. In the study the outline of the mechanistic modelling 
approach included elastic and viscoelastic layer properties, moving loads, the presence of 
fractured layers, imperfect interlayer bonding, and grid effects. To emulate the grid 
reinforcement, it was decomposed into three contributions namely the grid reinforcement being 
modelled as a thin layer consisting of a new material, the effect of the grid reinforcement on 
the interlayer bonding, and the Zone of Influence (ZoI) describing the grid influence on the 
properties of the surrounding asphalt concrete. The thin layer is a uniformly thick homogenous 
layer that is characterized by an effective modulus, Poisson’s ratio, and thickness. The effective 
thickness is found from considerations of the grid’s aperture geometry and actual thickness 
(not necessarily the same everywhere). When modelling the grid as a thin layer it is assumed 
that the grid completely separates the two adjacent layers, even though they will partially 
interact through the grid apertures. This leads to the grid being described by two bonding 
parameters, one for each of the layers. The ZoI is extrapolated from the ZoI caused by 
additional stress confinement, because of reinforcement, found in unbound granular layers. 
This extrapolation was done based on research that showed that stress confinement has a 
similar effect on AC under high temperatures and slow loading. A thickness that describes the 
range of influence of the ZoI is defined, and the aggregate characteristics are used to determine 
the size of the thickness. 
 
2.5 Summary of main findings  
The main finding of the review of literature was lack of investigations of tracked vehicles and 
vehicles with ribs. The few investigated vehicles were all a type of agricultural offroad vehicle. 
The studies examined were mostly trying to find the effect of the offroad vehicles on a flexible 
pavement (asphalt pavement), mostly in terms of comparing the pavement response with a 
standard vehicle’s pavement response. There was also tested for fatigue damage and pavement 
surface damage for some of the heavy offroad vehicles.  
 
2.5.1 Effect of offroad vehicles on flexible pavements 
For the findings of the effect of offroad vehicles on flexible pavements, the vehicles mainly 
found in the studies were agriculture offroad vehicles. These vehicles had either rubber tracks 
or ribbed flotation tires or both.  
The findings for these types of vehicles were done by different studies. For some agricultural 
vehicles, tests were done for different seasons resulting in responses obtained for different 
temperatures for the asphalt.  The tests were done for both empty and loaded agricultural 
vehicles. The empty vehicles were equal to or less damaging than the truck. The loaded vehicles 
were more damaging than the truck in the three tested seasons. 
For a study done on heavy offroad vehicles, they were tested on small rural roads. The damage 
of the offroad vehicles and responses were compared to a semi-truck. The results point to the 
surface damage depending on the asphalt pavement surface. On a road with surface dressing 
the vehicles made some indents and induced bleeding while nothing happened on a hot mix 
asphalt road without surface dressing. A pressure test found they caused no higher pressure in 
the pavement than other vehicles. A contact pressure test showed the agricultural vehicles 
==PAGE:16==
16 
 
caused no higher pressure than a tire. For the tracked vehicles it was also found it was the ribs 
under the rollers that transferred the load. 
A study constructed a mechanistic code that could calculate a pavement response, from a 
tracked vehicle’s different loading patterns, by using linear elastic theory with circular loading. 
The code uses many small loading circles and superposition of the solutions for the many 
loading circles, to approximate the loading from a rib of a flotation tire or track, to get a 
pavement response. The code was validated against pavement responses from tests conducted 
with agricultural vehicles. The results from a pavement analysis were that a standard semi-
truck and ESAL resulted in larger responses from the pavement systems considered, except for 
at the top of the subgrade where some of the agricultural vehicles resulted in higher stresses. 
The responses considered in the pavement analysis were the maximum tensile strain at the top 
and bottom of the asphalt layer, the maximum vertical stress at the top of the base layer and 
subgrade, and the maximum octahedral shear stress in the asphalt layer.  
  
2.5.2 Methods of field measurements 
The methods of field measurements are a broad topic with many different methods of gaining 
the response. In broad terms the measurement of the pavement is done by sensors embedded 
in the structure and subgrade (i.e., in-pavement sensing) or by a movable platform containing 
an array of sensors. In both cases, the sensors record the effects of a known load applied at the 
surface. 
From studies the general knowledge of some types of the instrumentation is gained. Pavement 
sensing is generally done to gain some sort of knowledge of the strength of the system, this can 
help predict its lifetime and quality. This can also be used to validate models and used for back 
calculating the moduli for the system. A normal test is a Falling weight Deflectometer test 
(FWD), which consists of a movable platform doing the pavement sensing. Other sensors that 
can be used for more permanent sensing, can be geophones, strain gauges, pressure cells, 
accelerometers, fiber optic sensors, and linear variable differential transformers (LVDT). These 
sensors can be used as embedded sensors or placed on the surface. These sensors have different 
advantages and disadvantages such as ranging precession.  All the different methods lead to 
the overall same conclusion, a measurement of the pavement response, which could be 
displacements, strains and or stresses.  
 
2.5.3 Asphalt reinforcement 
Asphalt reinforcement can be used to reduce the strains, within the AC. However, the 
reinforcement can also cause debonding between the adjacent layers. The debonding can cause 
an increase in the strains to occur instead of the intended reduction. Grid reinforcement can be 
modelled by splitting the effect into three contributions: (i) the grid as a thin strong layer 
separating the layers it is placed between, (ii) the effect of the grid on the bonding between 
layers, and (iii) the zone of influence i.e. the area effected in the asphalt concrete. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:17==
17 
 
3 Objectives & Methodology 
The main objective of this work is to develop and validate a computational model, for 
calculating mechanical responses in asphalt pavements, under the loading conditions exerted 
by off-road vehicles, equipped with tracks or flotation tires. A secondary objective is to 
compare the key responses from the computational model for the off-road vehicles and semi-
truck, with the key responses from an ESAL. 
The pavement is modelled as a layered elastic half-space. Asphalt grid reinforcement is 
modelled as a thin high-modulus elastic layer with a ZoI effect. Coding is done in a Matlab 
environment, with ALVA as a subroutine for solving a linear elastic system under a single load, 
uniformly applied over a circular area. Contact patches between pavement surface and tracks 
or flotation tires are assumed rectangular. Each patch is modelled with many small circular 
areas with a user-selected radius. To simulate a realistic vehicle movement/rolling, the patches 
are sequentially lifted and lowered onto the surface. Code verification efforts will be based on 
the ability to reproduce the same results as an analytical elastic half-space and results from an 
established layered elastic code. 
Code validation efforts will be based on the ability to reproduce field-measured strain responses 
generated by several passes of two different off-road vehicles and a semi-truck (for reference). 
Specifically, full-scale testing will be done on the DTU Smart Road, which has four different 
asphalt sections, three of which are reinforced with an asphalt grid. The asphalt layer in this 
experimental road contains built-in strain and temperature sensors in each of the sections. The 
off-road vehicles are: (i) an infantry fighting vehicle (IFV) equipped with tracks, (ii) a dump 
truck equipped with large flotation tires, and (iii) a semi-truck equipped with pneumatic tires. 
Model inputs include loading conditions obtained from static measurements and pavement 
properties obtained from separate field and lab investigations.  
The computational model is used to predict the key responses of the off-road vehicles, semi-
truck and ESAL. The key responses are computed for four different synthetic pavement 
systems, modelled after Danish standard road systems. The considered key responses are 
commonly used in design codes and used as a comparison between the four vehicles, at two 
different driving speeds. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:18==
18 
 
4 Field work/Experimental campaign 
4.1 Test setup (what exists) 
At DTU a smart road was constructed on a 100 m section of Henrik Dams Allé. This road was 
constructed by NCC, SHP and S&P, where S&P paid for the road construction. The road was 
constructed by S&P for the purpose of testing their carbon grid reinforcement in and under the 
asphalt layer. Another purpose was also to install sensors in the road to create an outdoor 
laboratory for research. 
The construction of the road was done by removing the asphalt layer and paving a new 150 
mm layer of asphalt concrete (AB 8 70/100). The new asphalt layer was constructed in three 
layers of 40 mm, 70 mm, and 40 mm. The road was constructed in two lanes with a warm joint 
and compacted during the paving. The joint had a different transverse position for each of the 
three layers. The total width of the road was about 8 m. 
The 100 m road has four different sections (0, 1, 2, and 3) that are 25 m each. Section 0 is the 
part closest to Kolonnevej and is without any reinforcement. The other three have 
reinforcement at different depths. The reinforcement grid is the S&P Carbophalt G 200/200 
product. Section 1, next to section 0, is reinforced at the bottom of the asphalt layer (at a depth 
of 150 mm). For section 2 the reinforcement is 110 mm below the surface and for section 3, 
the part closest to Nordvej, the reinforcement is 40 mm below the surface. These different 
placements were possible by paving 40 mm asphalt, then 70 mm and at last 40 mm again. 
The sensors consist of strain gauges and PT100 temperature sensors. The PT100 sensors are in 
the middle of each the section, 12.5 m to the next section (longitudinal direction) and 3.95 m 
from each curb (transverse direction). The PT100 sensors are in the centres of the arranged 
strain gauges. There are 12 PT100 sensors, three placed in every section at the depths: 40 mm, 
110 mm, and 150 mm. 
There are six strain gauges in each section, three that measure in the longitudinal direction and 
three in the transverse direction, see Figure 1. All 24 strain gauges are embedded at the bottom 
of the asphalt layers so at a depth of 150 mm. There was one extra strain gauge that was placed 
in section 3 (sensor 7) on top of the grid reinforcement and in the longitudinal direction. In 
section 1 sensor 2 did not work after the paving and in section 2 sensor 1 did not work. Both 
sensors measure strain in the longitudinal direction. The strain sensor model is: KYOWA KM-
120-120-H2-11.  
==PAGE:19==
19 
 
 
Figure 1 Illustration of the plan for the sensor placement and numbering for each section. It is the same distance between all 
the sensors. 
 
4.2 Preliminary tests 
4.2.1 Laboratory tests 
Previously a laboratory test was conducted on the AC, to acquire the master curve, which was 
then shared with this project. The master curve was obtained by drilling out two of cores, one 
from section 0 and one from section 2. They were tested using an indirect tensile test with a 
temperature interval of 0 to 35 °C and with a temperature step size of 5°C. The master curve 
can be seen in Figure 2. The shift parameters were found from the testing to be 𝑇0 = 20℃, 
𝐶1 = 23, and 𝐶2 = 197℃. The viscoelastic properties describing the master curve are          
𝐸0 = 30000 1/MPa, 𝐸∞= 100 1/MPa, 𝜏𝐷
0 = 47 s, and 𝑛𝐷= 0.47. 
 
Figure 2 Master curve for asphalt concrete at the reference temperature 20 °C. 
 
==PAGE:20==

4.2.2 FWD & DCP tests 
After the AC was milled but before it was repaved, a DCP test was conducted on each section 
on 04.10.2021. The layer thicknesses and moduli found from the DCP tests can be seen in 
Table 1. The maximum depth reached with the DCP test was 2000 mm. After the asphalt was 
repaved a FWD test was conducted on each section on 29.03.2022. The data from both the 
DCP and FWD tests, was obtained before the start of the project, and was then shared with this 
project afterwards.

The FWD test was performed in cold temperatures with the surface being between 5.36 °C and 
8.21 °C and the air temperature being between 6.5 °C and 8.2 °C during the testing. 
In each of the 4 sections drops were made in 5 locations, see Figure 3. Location 2, 3, and 4 
were chosen to be around the strain sensors in each section. At each of the 5 locations 6 drops 
were made, 2 with a pressure of about 0.8 MPa, 2 with a pressure of about 1.1 MPa, and 2 with 
a pressure of about 1.4 MPa. Section 1 location 5 had 2 additional drops with a pressure of 
about 0.3 MPa, while section 2 location 2 and section 3 location 5 both had 2 additional drops 
with a pressure of about 1.4 MPa (so 4 drops with about 1.4 MPa in each of the 2 locations).  
 
Figure 3 Illustration of placement for FWD drops. 
The thickness of the AC used in all 4 sections was 150 mm. The thicknesses of the base layers 
used in the calculations were based on the thicknesses found in the DCP test, see Table 1. The 
subgrade was modelled as having a semi-infinite thickness. In the 3 sections with grid 
reinforcement, it was chosen to not include the reinforcement as a layer in the backcalculations. 
==PAGE:21==
21 
 
Nielsen et al. (2022) showed that grid reinforcement had a negligible effect at low temperatures 
and high speeds, which both apply for the test conditions of the FWD measurements. Because 
of this, the AC layers and grid reinforcement are modelled as one (combined) homogenous 
layer that is characterized by an ‘equivalent’ modulus instead, which is also done by Graziani 
et al. (2014). 
The measured deflections were normalized to the pressures 0.3 MPa, 0.8 MPa, 1.1 MPa, or 1.4 
MPa depending on which is the closest to the actual pressure. When doing the backcalculation, 
the normalized deflections were averaged for each pressure at each location. The calculated 
deflections were found using the ELLEA1 program from Levenberg (2016) and using the 
Poisson’s ratio of 𝜈= 0.35 for all the layers. The absolute relative errors between the averaged 
measurements and the calculated deflections were calculated and minimized using the Solver 
in Excel. The moduli found for section 3 were 23723 MPa for the asphalt, 72 MPa for the base 
and 157 MPa for the subgrade. The asphalt modulus was much higher than the moduli for the 
other 3 sections, see Table 2. Because of this a computational test of the assumption that the 
grid reinforcement had a negligible effect on the FWD results was conducted in ALVA for 
section 3. In the test the surface deflection under the middle of the load plate was calculated 
for section 3 with and without the grid reinforcement. When including the grid reinforcement, 
the same parameters as in section 5.3.3 were used. The surface deflection, when excluding the 
reinforcement, is 0.4321 mm while it was 0.4261 mm when including it. This is a decrease of 
1.39% when including the grid, but the asphalt modulus for section 3 when compared to the 
average of the 3 other sections (20213 MPa) is 17.37% larger. The likelihood of the 
reinforcement causing the difference between the moduli is therefore very small. Based on this 
and the fact that it was the same asphalt that was used, and it was paved at the same time, the 
asphalt moduli should be approximately the same for all 4 sections. To take this into account 
the backcalculation was done once more for section 3 but where the asphalt modulus was fixed 
to be equal to the average of the other 3 moduli (20213 MPa). The moduli for all 4 sections are 
presented in Table 2. The found moduli were judged to not depend significantly on the actual 
pressure used for the drops. At the geophone distances, the average of the measured deflections, 
and the deflections calculated from the average moduli found in the backcalculation, are shown 
together in Figure 4, for section 0, and shown together in Figure 5, for section 3, while the same 
graphs for the other 2 sections are shown in appendix A.1.  
Table 2 The moduli for each layer in each section found from the backcalculation with the AC layer in section 3 being the 
average modulus of the other three sections.

Figure 4 Average of measured deflections, and deflections calculated from the average moduli found in the backcalculation, 
at geophone distances for section 0. 
 
Figure 5 Average of measured deflections, and deflections calculated from the average moduli found in the backcalculation, 
at geophone distances for section 3. 
When comparing the moduli found from the DCP tests (Table 1) and the backcalculation (Table 
2) the base moduli are lower for the backcalculation while the subgrade moduli are higher. 
When doing the backcalculation, the geophones closest to the centre of the load plate have the 
highest influence on the moduli of the top layers. The asphalt layer is relatively thin, and it is 
==PAGE:23==
23 
 
not possible to place geophones under the load plate (except at the centre), which leads to the 
backcalculation being imprecise at the top of the pavement. This might lead to the AC modulus 
“consuming” some of the base layer modulus. Another influence on the calculated base moduli 
was the low temperatures present during the FWD testing. The low temperatures cause the 
layers to be stiffer, which means the asphalt layer will transfer less stress to the base layer. 
Since the modulus of the base layer is dependent on the stress level (mentioned in Salazar 
Espinosa et al. 1975), the evaluated modulus will be lower. To take this into account the base 
modulus will be increased (more details written where it is implemented, see Chapter 5.3.4). 
When doing a backcalculation from a FWD test the found moduli are not necessarily exact, but 
instead an approximation. When combining this with the fact that DTU Smart Road is fairly 
short, and paved at the same time, it is reasonable to assume that the moduli would be the same 
for the AC and for the subgrade over the entire road section. The moduli that were chosen for 
the two layers are the average values found in the backcalculation. This leads to a modulus for 
the AC of 20200 MPa and for the subgrade of 165 MPa. The reason the base modulus is not 
assumed the same over the entire road section is because of the difference in layer thicknesses 
between section 0 and 1 and section 2 and 3. Instead it is assumed that the base modulus is the 
same for section 0 and 1 (for each vehicle) and is the same for section 2 and 3 (for each vehicle).  
 
4.3 Main experiment 
During Tuesday the 11th of October 2022 the weight and dimensions of the vehicles used were 
recorded, along with the horizontal strain responses, caused by them driving over DTU Smart 
Road, and the accompanying temperatures. The vehicles consisted of a dump truck, an infantry 
fighting vehicle (IFV), and a semi-truck. 
The dump truck used in the experiment was a Hydrema 922D seen in Figure 6, the semi-truck 
was a Volvo FMX 410 seen in Figure 7, and the IFV was a CV9035 seen in Figure 8.

4.3.2 Weighing of vehicles  
The procedure for weighing all the different vehicles was overall the same. VD provided 10 
Haenni standard weights (some of them stopped working during the day) that were used. The 
weights had a measuring accuracy of 20 kg. The weighing was done while the drivers were 
inside the vehicles which added some weight (estimate of an additional 80 to 100 kg). The 
location for the weighing was different for each of the 3 vehicles, see Figure 18 for the 
locations. 
The first vehicle to be weighed was the dump truck. The location of the weighing was relatively 
flat but a small downwards tilt towards the front of the vehicle was observed. The weight 
distribution for the dump truck is presented in Table 11. The second vehicle to be weighed was 
the semi-truck. The location of the weighing was on an asphalt road which was found to be 
flatter than where the dump truck was weighed. The dump truck was not weighed again because 
of time constraints. The semi-truck was weighed twice, the second time being turned 180 
degrees compared to the first weighing. This was done because a bigger difference in the weight 
of the wheels on the same axle was observed. If it was because of an uneven surface, this would 
mitigate the influence of the unevenness. The weight distribution for the semi-truck is 
presented in Table 12. The third and last vehicle to be weighed was the IFV. The location of 
the weighing was inside the experimental hall in building 119 at DTU which was very flat. A 
weight was placed underneath each of the 7 rollers on one side since there weren’t enough 
weights for the entire belt. This was done for both sides. When weighing both sides, the weights 
were only moved slightly in the direction along the vehicle, and this was only because the 
==PAGE:31==
31 
 
rollers don’t share an axle with the rollers on the other side of the vehicle. The weight 
distribution from this approach is for a scenario where the belt/track between the rollers do not 
touch the ground. This is not what happens in reality, but it is a close estimate and what was 
possible to measure. The weight distribution for the IFV is presented in Table 13.

4.3.3 Strain measurement procedure 
When measuring the strain and temperature, it was done for one section at a time. The first 
thing done in each section was to connect the sensors to a computer. The temperature 
measurements were taken continuously during the day. When switching sections this led to 
temperature readings of zero, very high temperatures, or very low temperatures which are 
obvious outliers when looking at the temperature data. For the IFV the AC temperature during 
testing was between 16 °C and 19 °C, while it was between 17 °C and 23 °C for the dump truck 
and semi-truck. 
Before using the strain gauges in each section, they needed to be warmed up before the 
measurements began. The reason for this is that the electrical current that begins to run through 
the gauges when taking measurements heats up and thereby expands the gauge. This can be 
seen on the measurements by an increase in strain when nothing is on the road. This increase 
will stop after a short measurement and the gauges can be reset to zero and are then ready to 
be used in the experiment. 
To find the transverse and longitudinal offset of the vehicles with respect to a known point, a 
patch of modelling clay was spread on the pavement surface. When the vehicles had made one 
pass an imprint would be left in the modelling clay from which the two offsets could be 
measured. The modelling clay was smoothed out between each pass, so the old imprints 
wouldn’t disturb the offset measurements, see Figure 19. The passes were also filmed from the 
side where the wheels or belt could be seen along with some of the pavement surface. A GoPro 
camera was also mounted on the side of all 3 vehicles, where the side of the wheel or belt and 
some of the pavement surface was visible. There was made markings on the pavement surface 
with spray paint and permanent markers indicating every 10 cm offset in the transverse 
direction. All 3 vehicles made all passes with their right side being in the middle of the road 
and driving in the left side of the road. 
 
Figure 19 Picture of modelling clay being smoothed out. 
All 3 vehicles had passes at a slow speed and a faster speed. All the slower passes for the dump 
truck and semi-truck had driving speeds between 3 km/h and 7 km/h, while the IFV had speeds 
between 6 km/h and 16 km/h. All the faster passes for the dump truck and IFV had driving 
speeds between 24 km/h and 38 km/h while the semi-truck had speeds between 15 km/h and 
19 km/h. The IFV had 3 passes at the slower speeds and 3 passes at the faster speeds, for each 
section. The dump truck and semi-truck on the other hand only had 2 passes, at each speed 
==PAGE:33==
33 
 
level, for each section, each. This was done because of time constraints. For all the passes the 
vehicles were driving forward, with the exception of the 2nd pass for the IFV, at section 3, 
where it reversed. 
The driving speeds of the vehicles for each of the passes was found by dividing the distances 
between the axles, with the time between the corresponding peaks on the strain response plots. 
This was done for multiple sensor measurements and an average of the speed was then taken. 
For the dump truck and the IFV there were multiple peaks for each sensor measurement. For 
the dump truck the time differences were taken both between the first and middle peak and 
between the middle and last peak, and the average speed included both readings. For the IFV 
the time differences were taken between peak number 3 and 6. 
It was observed from the imprint in the modelling clay and from the videos from the side that 
the belt of the IFV waved during the passes. This wave caused the rubber patches on the belt 
to move forward a little each time a roller passed over the patch. A picture of the modelling 
clay after a pass can be seen on Figure 20, while a video of the waving can be found in 
Appendix A.2. This is not taken into account when modelling the response. 
 
Figure 20 Modelling clay after pas 6 for the IFV in section 1. 
 
4.3.4 Offsets during testing 
On the day of the experiment 11.10.2022 the offsets were measured to a fixed point in the 
middle of the road marked on the asphalt in each section. For the longitudinal offsets that were 
measured, there were too many uncertainties for them to be useful. The transverse offsets were 
then calculated for each of the vehicles. For the IFV the offsets were calculated from the middle 
of the road to the middle of the patches on the right belt (when looking at the IFV from behind). 
For the dump truck the offsets were calculated from the middle of the road to the line D8 (see 
Figure 10) on the left patch on the right wheel. For the semi-truck the offsets were calculated 
from the middle of the road to the middle of the right outer rear wheel (see Figure 14). The 
==PAGE:34==
34 
 
transverse offsets for all three vehicles can be seen in Table 14. The sign convention is positive 
when the offset is to the left side of the middle of the road, when looking in the south direction 
(the driving direction for all passes, except pass 2 in section 3 for the IFV, where it reversed). 
 
Table 14 Transverse offsets from middle of the road. For the IFV it is to the middle of the patch on the right. For the dump 
truck it is to the line D8 (see Figure 10). For the semi-truck it is to the middle of the outer rear wheel. The sign convention is 
positive when the offset is to the left side of the middle of the road when looking in the south direction.

4.3.5 Strain measurements 
4.3.5.1 IFV 
4.3.5.1.1 Section 0 
Example measurements taken for the IFV in section 0 can be seen in Figure 21, Figure 22, 
Figure 23, Figure 24, Figure 25, and Figure 26. Figure 21 shows a cut out of the longitudinal 
strain response, while Figure 22 and Figure 23 show a cut out of the transverse strain responses, 
for pass 3, where the driving speed was 13.4 km/h. Figure 24, Figure 25, and Figure 26 show 
the same but for pass 4, were the driving speed was 26.1 km/h. There is a noticeable sensor 
drift for the transverse strain measurements (see Figure 22, Figure 23, Figure 25, and Figure 
26). When the strain measurements don’t go back to zero right away, some of it is cause by the 
AC being viscoelastic, but the strain measurements ‘plateau’ at a different value than zero, 
when letting the measurement continue after the vehicles have passed by. On top of that the 
difference is too large to just be the viscoelastic effects from the AC. 
For both passes all the longitudinal strain measurements have 7 troughs, which corresponds to 
the seven rollers on the IFV. For the transverse strain measurements sensor 5 also shows 7 
troughs, while sensor 4 and sensor 6 both show 7 peaks for both passes. There is a form of 
‘build up’ for the transverse strain measurements, that is not present for the longitudinal strain 
measurements. 
For both pass 3 and pass 4 the IFV drove closest to sensor 2 and sensor 5. This is seen on the 
size of the response, for these sensors, being bigger than all the other sensors. For pass 3 the 
IFV drove closer to sensor 1 and sensor 6 than to sensor 3 and sensor 4. This is again seen on 
the sizes of the responses. For pass 4 the IFV seems to have driven approximately right between 
sensor 1 and sensor 3 and between sensor 4 and sensor 6. This is seen by the responses being 
approximately the same size for sensor 1 and sensor 3 and for sensor 4 and sensor 6. 
When comparing the two passes the evolution of the troughs and peaks are the same. The 
strains measured by sensor 1 and sensor 4 both have approximately the same size for both 
passes. The strains measured by sensor 2, sensor 3 and sensor 5 are larger for pass 4 than pass 
3, while the strains measured by sensor 6 are a little larger for pass 3, than pass 4. The difference 
can be caused by the longitudinal placement of the patches on the track, compared to the 
sensors. There will be a higher response if the patch lands on top of the sensor compared to if 
the sensor lies between where two patches land. 
==PAGE:36==
36 
 
 
Figure 21 Longitudinal strain for the IFV on section 0 pass number 3. Driving speed of 13.4 km/h. 
 
 
Figure 22 Transverse strain for the IFV on section 0 pass number 3 for sensor 4 and sensor 6. Driving speed of 13.4 km/h.

4.3.5.1.2 Section 1  
An example of the pavement response for section 1 from the IFV can be seen in  
Figure 27 and Figure 28 for the longitudinal direction. The response for transverse direction 
can be seen on Figure 29 - Figure 31. For this pass the IFV was driving at a speed of 10.2 km/h. 
For this section sensor 2 is not working and therefore only two sensors work in the longitudinal 
direction.  
Similar to section 0, where there is no grid reinforcement, there can be seen 7 troughs/peaks 
corresponding to the seven rollers on each side that transfers the IFV weight to the pavement. 
The response measured from sensor 1 and 3 should be similar because the IFV was driving 
almost directly over sensor 2, which can be seen in Table 14.  
==PAGE:39==
39 
 
The response seen on Figure 27 and Figure 28 shows the response for sensor 1 and 3. The 
response is not the same. This could be because of noise/unwanted data in the response signal 
from sensor 3. The spread in the data could come from many different sources, one that was 
observed is, the vibration coming from the IFV, this could both be felt and heard, and the 
moving of the patch can be seen on Figure 20. Later in the report when validating, filtering of 
the data is done. There is seen the same tendency as section 0 where the third last trough is 
lower in value of around 8 microstrain. The two last troughs are then higher in value at around 
15-18 microstrain. The troughs show the assumption of stationary weight distribution is the 
same when driving is perhaps not completely correct. Also, with these low values when 
measuring the precision of the measuring equipment could have an influence on the measured 
data. 
The transverse strain gauges 4, 5, and 6 can be seen on Figure 29 - Figure 31. It shows a high 
response from sensor 5, with a trough around 165 microstrain, expected to be lower if the drift 
was not there. This response was anticipated since the sensor was driven almost directly over. 
There can be seen 7 troughs that indicate the rollers loading, similar to the longitudinal sensors. 
There can be seen a build-up of strain in the transverse sensor 5 similar to section 0, one cause 
can be because of how the IFV tracks work. The tracks start loading from the IFV front and 
the load around the sensor increases until max around the middle of the IFV and then begins 
decreasing again when it drives away.  
The Figure 27, Figure 28, and Figure 30 shows similar sensor drift again as for section 0. The 
sensors 1 and 3 has little to no drift and sensor 5 has around 35 microstrain drift. The Figure 
29 and Figure 31, sensor 4 and 6, also have a similar drift where the response slopes, this could 
be because of many reasons such as mechanical and the pavement material behaviour. 
 
 
Figure 27 Longitudinal strain for the IFV on section 1 pass number 1 for sensor 1. Driving speed of 10.2 km/h. 
==PAGE:40==
40 
 
 
Figure 28 Longitudinal strain for the IFV on section 1 pass number 1 for sensor 3. Driving speed of 10.2 km/h. 
 
 
Figure 29 Transverse strain for the IFV on section 1 pass number 1 for sensor 4. Driving speed of 10.2 km/h. 
 
==PAGE:41==
41 
 
 
Figure 30 Transverse strain for the IFV on section 1 pass number 1 for sensor 5. Driving speed of 10.2 km/h. 
 
 
Figure 31 Transverse strain for the IFV on section 1 pass number 1 for sensor 6. Driving speed of 10.2 km/h. 
 
An example of the measured response from a faster driving speed of 26.1 km/h for the IFV on 
section 1, can be seen in Figure 32-Figure 35. The longitudinal sensor response seen on Figure 
32 and Figure 33 has generally the same tendency as for the slower speed, there can be seen 7 
troughs again. Here was the IFV driving more towards the sensors 3 and 4, seen in table 14, 
than the sensors 1 and 6.  
This would result in a higher strain response for these sensors which is also what can be seen 
from Figure 32 and Figure 33. Because of the spread in the data, the maximum/minimum value 
cannot be determined exact. The spread source is expected to be similar to what is explained 
for the slower driving. For sensor 1 the strain is around 5-10 microstrain. For sensor 3 it around 
25-40 microstrain. 
==PAGE:42==
42 
 
For the transverse strain sensors seen in Figure 34 and Figure 35 shows a similar response as 
the slower speed response in Figure 29-Figure 31, but sensor 4 shows a larger response because 
of the driving offset. The response from sensors 4 and 5 shows the same kind of build up as for 
the slow speed response. The sensor 5 also have drift similar to the slower pass, the drift is 
around 10 microstrain. There is also a small amount of drift seen on sensor 4. The sensor 6 also 
shows a similar response drift as for what is seen on sensor 4 and 6 from the slower driving 
response.  
Overall, the peaks are more difficult to distinguish, but there is still the indication of the loading 
from each roller. The maximum strain from sensor 4, 5 and 6 respectively is around:  45 
microstrain, 62 microstrain and 26 microstrain.

4.3.5.1.3 Section 2 
Figure 36 to Figure 39 shows the measurement for section 2 when the IFV drove with a speed 
of 10.4 km/h. Sensor 1 in this section does not work and therefore there is one less longitudinal 
strain measurement for this section. 
Figure 36 shows measurements for sensor 2. It is observed that there are seven troughs 
corresponding to each of the seven rollers passing by sensor 2. The results are shifted a little 
so that they do not start and end in zero but rather somewhere around 10.  As for previous 
measurements some sensor drift is observed, however not in a concerning magnitude. The 
results seem to ocellate, this is because of the rollers being so close together that their individual 
responses interfere with one another.    
 
Figure 36 Longitudinal strain for the IFV on section 2 pass number 1 for sensor 2. Driving speed of 10.4 km/h. 
Figure 37 shows the measurements for sensor 3. There seems to be some additional noise for 
this sensor which makes it very difficult to interpret the results. Unfortunately, this was the 
case with this sensor for all six passes with the IFV. It is still possible to notice the seven 
troughs, but it is not easy to comment on sensor drift or read the actual strain from this plot.
 
Figure 37 Longitudinal strain for the IFV on section 2 pass number 1 for sensor 3. Driving speed of 10.4 km/h. 
==PAGE:45==
45 
 
Figure 38 shows the results for sensor 5. The seven troughs are not as clear as for the 
longitudinal measurements. The results do not seem to oscillate around some value as for the 
longitudinal, but instead they seem to build up over time which is characteristic for the 
transverse measurements. As observed for previous transverse measurements there is a lot of 
sensor drift, about 50 micro strains in this case. 
 
Figure 38 Transverse strain for the IFV on section 2 pass number 1 for sensor 5. Driving speed of 10.4 km/h. 
Figure 39 shows the results for both sensor 4 and 6. These two are very similar in both shape 
and size. For this pass the IFV drove very close to sensor 5 which lies between 4 and 6 so it 
makes sense that these looks very similar but a little shifted in time. They both seems to show 
seven peaks instead of troughs. Both sensors seem to have a lot of drift relative to their 
magnitude. 
 
Figure 39 Transverse strain for the IFV on section 2 pass number 1 for sensor 4 and sensor 6. Driving speed of 10.4 km/h. 
 
 
==PAGE:46==
46 
 
Figure 40 to Figure 42 show the measured strains for when the IFV drove with a speed of 37.0 
km/h over section 2. 
Figure 40 shows the longitudinal strain measurements. Sensor 2 has seven troughs as expected, 
but the response from sensor 3 is very difficult to read. The response seems to “drown” in the 
noise, and it is mostly not even possible to detect peaks and troughs. 
 
Figure 40 Longitudinal strain for the IFV on section 2 pass number 6 for sensor 2 and sensor 3. Driving speed of 37.0 km/h. 
Figure 41 show the measurements for sensor 4. The response is quite small indicating that the 
IFV did not drive close by the sensor. The number of peaks is difficult to tell. They seem to 
melt a bit together and are less notable than for previous measurements. The measurements 
seem to be in compression, but it has a very large sensor drift relative to its size which makes 
it difficult to tell. 
 
Figure 41 Transverse strain for the IFV on section 2 pass number 6 for sensor 4. Driving speed of 37.0 km/h. 
 
 
==PAGE:47==
47 
 
Figure 42 shows the measurements for sensor 5 and 6. Just as for sensor 4, the troughs seem to 
melt together making it difficult to distinguish them from each other. Here the responses are in 
tension. Sensor 5 has only a small sensor drift while sensor 6 has a large one, though not as 
much compared to its size as sensor 4. The IFV drove closest to sensor 6 and therefore the 
absolute strains are larger.  
 
Figure 42 Transverse strain for the IFV on section 2 pass number 6 for sensor 5 and sensor 6. Driving speed of 37.0 km/h. 
 
4.3.5.1.4 Section 3 
Example measurements taken for the IFV in section 3 can be seen in Figure 43 to Figure 49. 
Figure 43 and Figure 44 show a cut out of the longitudinal and transverse strain respectively 
for pass 1 where the driving speed was 7.8 km/h. Figure 45, Figure 46, Figure 47, Figure 48, 
and Figure 49 show the same but for pass 5 were the driving speed was 32.4 km/h. The IFV 
drove closest to sensor 2 and 5 for both pass 1 and 5. This is seen by the strain response being 
bigger for these sensors. In pass 5 the IFV was a little closer to sensor 3 and sensor 4 than 
sensor 1 and sensor 6. This is again seen on the size of the peaks and troughs.  
For the longitudinal strain there are 7 distinct peaks or troughs for all the strain responses except 
for sensor 1 for pas 5 (Figure 45). The 7 peaks or troughs correspond to the 7 rollers for the 
IFV. There are 7 peaks or troughs for each sensor for pas 1 in the transverse direction (Figure 
44), although some of the peaks/troughs can be hard to distinguish at a glance. This is especially 
true for the 1st trough for sensor 5. The peaks or troughs are even harder to distinguish on 
Figure 48 and Figure 49. It is probably only for sensor 6, where 7 peaks would be identified, if 
the number of peaks/troughs and the approximate distance between them, was unknown. Just 
like in section 0, sensor drift is observed on the transverse strain readings. There is a form of 
‘build up’ for the transverse strain measurements, that is not present for the longitudinal strain 
measurements. The longitudinal strain responses have a shape similar to oscillations around a 
zero-line. The transverse strain responses also have a shape similar to oscillations, but this is 
around a curved or angled line. 
For Figure 44 and Figure 48 and Figure 49 the overall shapes of the strain responses are the 
same, except for sensor 4. In pass 5 the IFV passed closer by sensor 4 leading to the higher 
response seen in Figure 48. The response shape for sensor 4 for pass 5 is therefore a mixture 
==PAGE:48==
48 
 
of the response shapes seen for sensor 5 and sensor 6. For sensor 6 the responses are 
approximately equal in size. For sensor 5 the strain response is bigger for pass 1 compared to 
pass 5.  
When comparing the longitudinal strain responses on Figure 43 with the responses on Figure 
45, Figure 46, and Figure 47 the overall shape is the same, except for the 1st and 7th troughs. 
The troughs are bigger for pass 1, when comparing the size to the other troughs for the same 
pass. When comparing the sizes of the troughs, pass 1 has the biggest peaks for sensor 2, but 
pass 5 has the biggest troughs for sensor 1 and 3. A difference other than the driving speed 
between the two passes are the transverse offsets. There could also be a difference in where the 
patches land compared to the sensors. The measured response will be higher when the sensor 
lies under the patch instead of between them. 
 
Figure 43 Longitudinal strain for the IFV on section 3 pass number 1. Driving speed of 7.8 km/h.

4.3.5.2 Dump truck 
4.3.5.2.1 Section 0 
An example of the measured response for pass 1 in section 0 for the dump truck is shown on 
Figure 50, Figure 51, and Figure 52. The dump truck was driving 5.2 km/h. The transverse 
driving offset, seen on table 14, results in the dump truck mostly driving over sensor 2 and 
sensor 5.  
The response seen on the Figure 50 for the longitudinal sensors 1-3 shows three troughs which 
is expected from the three axles on the vehicle. It can also be seen that sensor 2 produces the 
largest response. The responses from sensor 1 and 3 have similar magnitudes, which indicates 
that the dump truck was driving with close to equal distance to the sensors, and therefore over 
sensor 2. The size of the troughs also seems to follow the expected weight distribution. The 
front axle weighed the most, with the middle axle weighing a little more than the rear axle. 
The transverse sensors 4-6, on Figure 51 and Figure 52, show a three axle response as 
expected. For Figure 51 the troughs are close to the same height. In between the troughs the 
strain does not revert back to the starting value with the difference being at around 30 
microstrain and 50 microstrain. There can be many reasons for this, some that come to mind 
are tire/load interaction and sensor drift. Sensor drift is seen after the last trough, the response 
does not regress to the starting value. For Figure 52 the response for sensor 4 and 6 shows 
peaks instead of troughs, except for the first tire response at around 20 microstrain for sensor 
4, where there can also be seen ripples/fluctuations. The response shows the sensors are 
mostly experiencing compression. There seems to be little drift for sensor 4 and 6 mostly in 
between the loading.

pass 2 the dump truck was closer to sensor 1 and sensor 6 than it was for pass 4. There are 3 
distinct troughs or peaks for all the sensor readings. This corresponds to the 3 wheels.  
For sensor 6 for pass 2 (Figure 67) the first trough is a bigger than the other two troughs. This 
is caused by the front wheel weighing more than the other two wheels. The size of the strains 
combined with their shape indicates that sensor 6 lies in the transition zone between tensile and 
compressive strains, for the middle and rear axle. Since sensor 4 lies further away (in the 
transverse direction) from the dump truck, the strains have transitioned into compressive 
strains. The same is seen for pass 4, where the strain caused by the middle and rear wheel is 
compressive for sensor 6. All the wheels cause a compressive strain for sensor 4. 
When comparing the two passes, the strains measured by sensor 1, sensor 2, sensor 4 and sensor 
5 are bigger for pass 2. For sensor 3 the first trough is bigger for pass 2, but the last two troughs 
are bigger for pass 4. For sensor 3 the difference between the trough sizes for the two passes is 
small, especially for the last two troughs. For sensor 6 the first trough is bigger for pass 2, while 
the other two troughs are peaks for pass 4. A reason, the response for both sensor 4 and the first 
trough of sensor 6, is bigger for pass 2 than for pass 4, even though the transverse offset would 
be larger, for at least one of the sensors, for pass 2, might be because the driving speed was 
lower for pass 2. The lower driving speed means the effective modulus for the AC will be lower 
because the AC is a viscoelastic material.
