4.3.5.4 Comparison between vehicles and sections 
When comparing the shape of the strain responses for the different vehicles, it is seen that there 
is a lot of interaction between the roller responses for the IFV, compared to the interaction 
between the responses from the axles for both the dump truck and the semi-truck. For the IFV 
the longitudinal strain responses have a shape similar to oscillations around a zero-line. The 
transverse strain responses also have a shape similar to oscillations, but this is around a curved 
or angled line. For the dump truck there is a larger interaction between the middle and rear 
wheel, than the front and middle wheel. This is because of the difference in distance between 
the axles. The smaller distance between rollers, is also the reason there is more interaction 
between the roller responses for the IFV. For the semi-truck there is only a slight to no 
interaction between the two axle responses. There will be interaction between the inner and 
==PAGE:74==
74 
 
outer rear wheel, because of the small distance between them. For all three vehicles, a larger 
sensor drift was observed for the transverse strains, than for the longitudinal strains. 
When trying to compare the strain responses for the different sections (for the same vehicle), 
the big differences in the pavement systems are the grid reinforcement and differences in the 
base layer. Overall, they are quite similar in appearance and size of the strain response, but 
there are some differences in the responses for the sections. A variable that would lead to 
differences in responses, is the nonidentical driving offset to the sensors and the uncertainty of 
the sensor placement. The layer thicknesses and moduli for the layers are also not entirely the 
same, and this can also result in differences in response and many other possibilities could also 
lead to this. This includes the differences from the grid reinforcement. So overall, there cannot 
be concluded on any specific reason, such as the grid reinforcement, without further 
investigation. There are too many uncertainties that condemn the comparison between the 
sections, from just looking at the measured responses. 
 
 
==PAGE:75==
75 
 
5 Mechanistic code 
5.1 Description of code 
5.1.1 Intro 
The code for this work was written in MATLAB and uses the LET module in the open-source 
software package ALVA (developed by Skar et al. 2020a).  ALVA is used as a subroutine for 
solving the linear elastic system under a single uniform circular load as described in 
Khazanovich and Wang (2007). This means that the code follows the same assumptions as 
described in Chapter 2.3. 
 
 
Figure 88 Coordinate system and stress/strain sign convention (From Poulos and Davis 1974) 
The code follows the coordinate system seen in Figure 88. Furthermore, tension is calculated 
as negative while compression is positive. The vehicle drives along the x-axis.  
The contact patches between the pavement and the vehicle are assumed to be rectangular and 
identical along the path. The patch can be rotated with an angel around its center. The load of 
the vehicles is modeled as many micro circles arranged in a grid such that they fill out the 
rectangular patch. The micro circles touch but do not overlap. 
To simulate the movement of the vehicles, it is assumed that the patches are sequentially lifted 
from and lowered onto the pavement surface. This is done by modeling a long row of patches 
and then gradually increasing and decreasing the load on each of the patches. The load is 
assumed to be transferred linearly from one load step to another. It is assumed that the total 
load of the vehicle is constantly distributed with 50% on the right side and 50% on the left side. 
It is also assumed that the load from each tire or roller is constant. 
5.1.2 Input 
For the mechanistic code the following inputs are needed. 
• Velocity of the vehicle  
• Pitch distances between contact patches. 
• Depth of the evaluation point. 
==PAGE:76==
76 
 
• Number of contact patches along the path. 
• Radius of the micro circles. 
• Length of each unique contact patch. 
• Width of each unique contact patch. 
• Angel rotated around its own center for each unique contact patch. 
• x-distance from the evaluation point to each unique contact patch. 
• y-distance from the evaluation point to each unique contact patch. 
• Distance between unique patch and corresponding patch in the opposite side of the 
vehicle. 
• Load history of contact patches. 
• Number of load steps in between patches. 
• Layer thicknesses. 
• Elastic moduli of layers. 
• Horizontal spring stiffness to describe bonding between layers 
• Poisson’s ratio of layers 
• Bessel zero points and Gauss points for Bessel functions. 
It was assumed that the load from the vehicle does not shift between right and left, and that the 
load of each roller or tire is constant. This means that the load history must be the same for all 
contact patches along the path, just shifted in time. This means that the code only needs the 
load history for one patch. A number of timesteps between patches must be chosen and the 
load history should be a vector that corresponds to the chosen timesteps between patches. This 
means that the load history and timesteps must be chosen in such a way, that they ensure that 
the sum of the load of all patches in one side of the vehicle, must give half of the total load at 
any time. The example vector below follows the load distribution shown in Figure 89 with one 
timestep between patches. 
𝑃= [0   𝑃1   0   0   𝑃2   𝑃3   0] 
(18) 
 
Figure 89 Illustration of possible load distribution corresponding to the vector in equation (18). 
The input must also contain a vector that indicates how much of the load from the load history 
goes to each of the patches.  
 
𝐹1 
𝐹2 
𝐹3

5.1.3 Procedure 
5.1.3.1 ALVA Subroutine 
The first thing the script does is to run the subroutine in ALVA. If ALVA were utilized to 
calculate for all micro circles in all the contact patches along the path of the vehicle, the runtime 
of the code would be quite long. Therefore, ALVA is only activated for a fixed amount of 
evaluation points along a line. The contribution from each micro circle at the evaluation point 
on the pavement, can then later be found by interpolating between the fixed points from ALVA.  
The ALVA subroutine is run with a load of 𝑞= 1 MPa. Since the model is linear elastic, it is 
fairly easy to correct for the load later in the code by simply multiplying the responses with the 
actual load divided by 𝑞. 
5.1.3.2 Sample patch 
The MATLAB function (Circ_coords.m) is used by the mechanistic code and is a slightly 
modified version of the function filling_function.m from Hesthaven (2021).  
After ALVA, the main script calls on the function Circ_coords.m for each unique patch. First 
the function calculates the coordinates, for the micro circles, for one sample patch relative to 
the patch center and then adds an offset so that the coordinates are relative to the evaluation 
point (0, 0, z). 
First in this function the boundaries of the patch are defined based on the values given in the 
input ark. 
To set up the center coordinates for the micro circles in a way such that the circles do not 
overlap, the step size between them is set to two times the radius a. 
𝑑= 2𝑎 
(19) 
Then the circle coordinates for the are set up in two vectors. 
𝑥𝑐= [−𝐿
2 + 𝑎,
−𝐿
2 + 𝑎+ 𝑑,
−𝐿
2 + 𝑎+ 2𝑑, ⋯       𝐿
2 −𝑎] 
(20) 
𝑦𝑐= [−𝑊
2 + 𝑎,
−𝑊
2 + 𝑎+ 𝑑,
−𝑊
2 + 𝑎+ 2𝑑, ⋯       𝑊
2 −𝑎] 
(21) 
Where 𝑥𝑐 and 𝑦𝑐 are the x and y coordinates for the circles, 𝐿 is the length of the patch and 𝑊 
is the width. 
If the size of the patch and size of the micro circles does not match op, the number of circles 
is rounded down and the center of the cluster of circles is moved to the center of the patch. 
Because all the micro circles fit within the rectangular patch the total area of the circles are 
always smaller than the area of the patch. This means that the pressure in the micro circles must 
be larger than the estimated pressure for the patch to obtain the correct total load. Because of 
this a fill ratio is defined as the area of the patch divided with the total area of the micro circles. 
𝐹𝑟=
𝐿𝑊
𝜋𝑎2𝑁𝑐

Where 𝐹𝑟 is the fill ratio and 𝑁𝑐 is the number of micro circles within the patch.

𝐶𝑐𝑜𝑜𝑟𝑑𝑠= [

(23)

𝑅= [cos(𝜃)

the evaluation point for the patch closest to the evaluation point.   
The output from this function that will be used in the main script is the final coordinates 𝑥𝑐𝑝 
and 𝑦𝑐𝑝, the fill ratio 𝐹𝑟 and the number of circles in a patch 𝑁𝑐.  Note that the output 
coordinates from this function are in cartesian coordinates.

After the function Circ_coords.m has run, the circle coordinates for all the contact patches 
along the chosen path are calculated.

𝑦𝑎𝑥𝑙𝑒 is the distance between two tires on the same axel or the distance between the two tracks 
depending on the vehicle. 
After all positions for all micro circles in all contact patches for both sides of the vehicle are 
found in cartesian coordinates, the radial distance from each micro circle to the evaluation point 
is calculated together with is angel 𝛼 to the x-axis. 
The fixed points used in ALVA and their corresponding mechanical responses are then used to 
find the response at the evaluation point for each micro circle in cylindrical coordinates by 
interpolation. The interpolation is carried out by the MATLAB function interp1, using either 
linear or spline interpolation depending on which was chosen in the input.

5.1.3.6 Principal responses 
The principal response in 3D, can be found by solving an eigenvalue problem for the response, 
here shown for the stress: 
[
𝜎𝑥𝑥−𝜎
𝜎𝑥𝑦
𝜎𝑥𝑧
𝜎𝑦𝑥
𝜎𝑦𝑦−𝜎
𝜎𝑦𝑧
𝜎𝑧𝑥
𝜎𝑧𝑦
𝜎𝑧𝑧−𝜎
] [
𝑛𝑥
𝑛𝑦
𝑛𝑧
] = [
0
0
0
]
(43) 
The solution can be found by calculating the determinant of the stress component matrix equal 
to 0 and solving for the roots. This can be done by MATLAB’s eig function, that calculates the 
eigenvalues, that are the principal stresses, and the eigenvectors, that are the orientation vectors. 
The principal stresses are ordered as follows 𝜎1 > 𝜎2 > 𝜎3. 
When analysing rutting failure, the maximum octahedral shear stress is needed (Wang et al. 
2009). When using the principal coordinate system, the octahedral shear stress can be 
calculated by: 
𝜏𝑜𝑐𝑡= 1
3 ⋅√(𝜎1 −𝜎2)2 + (𝜎2 −𝜎3)2 + (𝜎3 −𝜎1)2 
(44) 
The principal strain in 2D can be found by solving the eigenvalue problem for two dimensions: 
[𝜀𝑥−𝜀
𝜀𝑥𝑦
𝜀𝑦𝑥
𝜀𝑦−𝜀] [𝑛𝑥
𝑛𝑦] = [0
0] 
(45) 
This can be solved with the MATLAB function eig as described above. The principal strains 
are ordered as follows 𝜀1 > 𝜀2.

For the verification the chosen rectangular loading area has the dimensions l=100 and w=400. 
The uniform load p=1 MPa. The comparison between the code and the theory is done by 
calculating for different amounts of load circles ranging from 4 to 2500, see Figure 90. This is 
done to see how good of an approximation it is to use load circles to approximate the load area. 
The uniform loading from the circles needs to multiplied by a factor, that takes into account 
that the circles do not fully fill out the rectangular loading area as stated before. For a perfect 
fill this is 
4
𝜋≈1.27, which means the loading from a circle is 𝑝𝑐= 1.27 MPa. 
 
 
 
 
Figure 90 Illustration of the different amounts of load circles filling out the rectangle used in the verification. 
 
The comparison is done by looking at the absolute relative error: 
𝐸𝑎𝑏𝑠= |𝑐𝑇−𝑐𝐶|
𝑐𝑇
 
(51) 
Where 𝑐𝑇 is the result from the theory and 𝑐𝐶 is the result from the code. 
  
When comparing the analytical half-space displacement solutions to the mechanistic code it is 
done at different depths, for loading circle radii of: 𝑟= 50 mm (4 circles), 𝑟= 10 mm (100 
circles), 𝑟= 5 mm (400 circles), and 𝑟= 2 mm (2500 circles). The E-modulus is chosen to be 
10000 MPa and the Poisson’s ratio is 0.35. (Calculated with two layers for the code, with the 
first z=1000 mm and the second z=inf). The chosen interpolation is MATLAB interp1 spline 
interpolation. 
 
The verification between the code and analytical half-space solutions for the calculated 
displacement in the z-direction can be seen in Table 15, Table 16, Table 17, and Table 18. It 
==PAGE:83==
83 
 
can be seen that an overall tendency of having more loading circles results in lower errors. It 
can also be seen that near and at the top of the layer there seem to be higher errors. It also 
seems that at the corner, the results have a higher error overall compared to the middle 
calculations. This can be because of the circle loading results in the corner not being within 
the loading circles as can be seen on the Figure 90 above. 
It was also found from the comparison that there is a point where the amount of loading circles 
would not change the precision of the calculations in amount that would not be worth it for the 
extra computational time. This would be expected to be around 100-400 circles, but even the 
lowest amount tested for, 4 circles, gives a relatively good result with the highest error of 7.8%. 
The number of circles also seem to be less important if the evaluation point is at distance above 
200 mm away/in depth since the error is low.

For the second part of the verification the mechanistic code is compared to a similar linear 
elastic theory based Excel program ELLEA1. The code and ELLEA1 will use the same half-
space simulation input and the same 3-layered system for comparing the strain calculation by 
the absolute relative error (51) again. The half-space simulation can also be used to see if the 
interface conditions hold up if the calculation have higher errors after an interface. The systems 
can be seen in Figure 91.  
   
Figure 91 The half-space and 3-layered system for the verification. 
 
For comparing the Excel program ELLEA1 strain results, for 4 load circles, to the mechanistic 
code, also with 4 circles, at different evaluation points these cases are considered: 
1. At the middle (x=0 mm, y=0 mm) of loading area at different depths, z, for both the 
simulated half-space and the 3-layer elastic system. 
2. At the middle (y=0 mm) of loading area at different offsets in the x-direction at constant 
depth z=150 mm, for both the simulated half-space and the 3-layer elastic system. 
3. At a y=400 mm offset to the loading area at different offsets in the x-direction at 
constant depth z=150 mm, for both the simulated half-space and the 3-layer elastic 
system. 
 
For case 1 the results can be seen in Table 19, Table 20, and Table 21. The overall tendency 
shows small errors, thereby small differences. It seems the highest error is close to the top of 
the layer and at the deepest depth, so far away from the loading.

For case 2, the results can be seen on Table 22, Table 23, Table 24, and Table 25. Here again 
the error is mostly low (<1%). There seems to be a tendency of the error increasing the further 
away the evaluation point is in the x-offset. There can also be seen some big errors for 10000 
mm, which mostly comes from the difference between small amounts of strain and a difference 
in sign for low value. It can also be observed there are larger error for 𝜀𝑥𝑧 in Table 23, the strain 
values are also small and thereby is the error not that important. It seems that small absolute 
microstrain values (|𝜇𝜀| < 1) sometimes have higher errors, which could be because of 
numerical precision, from the interpolation and numerical integration and so on, which can 
have a bigger impact on this.

For case 3, the results can be seen in Table 26, Table 27, Table 28, Table 29, Table 30, and 
Table 31. Here again the error is mostly low (<1%). There seems to be a tendency of the error 
increasing the further away the evaluation point is in the x-offset from the loading area. There 
can also be seen some big errors for 10000 mm, but again it is for low strains. It can also be 
observed there are larger errors compared to the other calculated strain for 𝜀𝑥𝑧 and 𝜀𝑦𝑧 in Table 
30 and Table 31, the strain is small and thereby is the error not that important. It seems that 
small absolute microstrain values (|𝜇𝜀| < 1) sometimes have higher errors, which could be 
because of numerical precision, from the interpolation and numerical integration and so on, 
which has a bigger impact on this.

5.3 Validation 
To make sure that the code is able to reasonably recreate the pavement responses caused by an 
IFV, a dump truck, and a semi-truck, the code is validated against the measured strain responses 
described in Chapter 4.3.5. It is chosen to not present the validation of the faster passes since 
the responses have similar appearances and magnitudes to the slower passes. The decision was 
also made because of the computational time and the amount of data already present for the 
slower passes. The modulus for the AC is 20200 MPa and for the subgrade is 165 MPa. 
 
5.3.1 Temperature correction 
The FWD test was performed in cold temperatures with the surface being between 5.3 °C and 
8.3 °C and the air temperature being between 6.5 °C and 8.2 °C during the testing. During the 
testing with the three vehicles the AC temperature was between 16.7 °C and 18.4 °C for the 
IFV, while it was between 17.7 °C and 22.7 °C for the dump truck and semi-truck. Because of 
the temperature difference a correction of the AC modulus is made. To make the correction the 
master curve found in laboratory tests is used (see Chapter 4.2.1). To find a representative time 
the asphalt modulus found in the backcalculation is used (see Chapter 4.2.2). Since the modulus 
(20200 MPa) is an average for sections 0, 1, and 2 the average testing temperature for these 
sections (7.5 °C) is used for the master curve. The time corresponding to 20200 MPa at a 
temperature of 7.5 °C is then found. This time is then used to find the moduli from the master 
curve at different temperatures. This process is illustrated on Figure 92. 
==PAGE:93==
93 
 
 
 
Figure 92 Illustration of the temperature correction process. 
For each of the vehicles in each of the sections the average temperature for the three 
temperature sensors can be seen in Table 32. The temperature gradients are approximated by 
second-degree polynomials, which are then used to find the asphalt temperatures at any depth. 
The asphalt is modelled as one layer with a modulus based on the temperature at the middle. 
When including the grid reinforcement and ZoI (see Chapter 5.3.3) the temperature is chosen 
at the middle of each of the divisions.

5.3.2 Speed correction 
Asphalt concrete is a viscoelastic material, but it is modelled as an elastic material. For 
viscoelastic materials the length of the time loads are applied has an influence on the modulus 
which directly correlates to the speed of the vehicle. A correction factor is introduced to include 
the effect of the driving speed. For Danish conditions it is traditionally assumed that the 
correction factor R found in Vejregler (2022) can be used for speeds below 60 km/h. 
𝑅= (𝑉 [km/h]
60 km/h)
0.37
 
(52) 
This is an empirical formula that is made for pneumatic tires. To take into account that the IFV 
and the dump truck do not have pneumatic tires, a ratio between how long a point is loaded 
with the different tires/tracks compared to a pneumatic tire, with the same driving speed V, is 
found. If 𝛥 is the length of the contact area for a pneumatic tire, then the time a point is loaded 
when driving over it can be found by: 
𝑡1 = 𝛥1
𝑉 
(53) 
Since the driving speed is kept constant when finding the ratio then: 
𝑡1
𝑡2
= 𝛥1
𝛥2
 
(54) 
When making the load history for the IFV and the dump truck, it was assumed that the load is 
transferred from one patch to another linearly and that a minimum of 1 patch is loaded at all 
times. This means that a patch starts being loaded right after the patch before it reaches 
maximum load and stops being loaded when the patch after reaches maximum load. Because 
of this the effective length 𝛥2 for the IFV and the dump truck corresponds to 2 ⋅𝑝𝑖𝑡𝑐ℎ. The 
pneumatic tire is assumed to have 𝛥1 = 0.3 m. This leads to the IFV having a ratio of 0.97 and 
the dump truck having a ratio of 0.73. As an example, if the dump truck drove with 10 km/h 
then the speed correction factor would be: 
𝑅= (0.73 ⋅10 km/h
60 km/h
)
0.37
= 0.46 
(55) 
 
5.3.3 Reinforcement grid implementation 
The way the reinforcement grid is implemented in the code is through the input parameters 
(implementation based on Nielsen et al. 2022). The input parameters are based on Nielsen et 
al. (2022) and S&P (Carbophalt G 200/200). The modulus for the grid is 265,000 MPa, the 
Poisson’s ratio is 0.15, and with an average cross-sectional area per unit width of 50 mm2/m 
the effective (smeared) thickness is 0.05 mm. The effective aperture size (EAS) is 15 mm. The 
AC’s nominal maximum aggregate size (NMAS) is 8 mm. This leads to the EAS-NMAS ratio 
being 1.9 which is well within the representative volume element (RVE) limit of about 5. It is 
assumed that 𝜅= 2, and that the ZoI thickness is 𝛿𝑍𝑜𝐼= 3 ⋅NMAS = 24 mm. The ZoI is only 
applied to the AC so in section 1 the ZoI only extends upwards. It is assumed that there is 
perfect bonding between the AC layers and the reinforcement grid since it is not possible to 
measure the bonding parameter for DTU Smart Road.  
==PAGE:95==
95 
 
 
5.3.4 Base modulus and sensor placement 
The base moduli and the sensor placements are fitted to the strain responses. The base moduli 
differ for each vehicle since the base modulus is dependent on the stress level (mentioned in 
Salazar Espinosa et al. 1975), they are presented in Table 33. The distance from the middle of 
the road to each of the sensors in each section can be seen in Table 34. Since sensor 2 in section 
1 and sensor 1 in section 2 did not work a distance was not found for these.

For some of the sections the entire sensor configuration was moved (in the transverse 
direction). For section 2 the sensor configuration was moved 20 mm. This corresponds well 
with Figure 39, where the response indicates that the IFV drove over the middle of the sensor 
configuration. The figure shows pass 1 in section 2, which had an offset of 2 cm as seen in 
Table 14. For section 3 there is an offset of 70 mm on the sensor configuration. This 
corresponds well with Figure 43 and Figure 44, where the response indicates that the IFV drove 
over the middle of the sensor configuration. The figures show pass 1 in section 3, which had 
an offset of 6 cm as seen in Table 14. The movement of the entire configuration is already 
included in the distances in Table 34. 
When measuring the offsets in the modelling clay, there was a measuring uncertainty caused 
by the imprints of the wheels overlapping (all the wheels passing over the same spot). There is 
also an uncertainty in whether or not the imprints are slightly larger than the patches, since the 
patches could push the modelling clay a bit further away from their edge. Because of these 
uncertainties, some of the offsets for specific passes were allowed a change of up to ±20 mm. 
For the dump truck this allowable change was up to -40 mm instead, because of the rough 
estimate of the patches. In section 0 the change was done for pass 1 for the semi-truck where 
the offset was changed with -10 mm. The change was also done for pass 3 for the IFV where 
the offset was changed with -10 mm. In section 1, pass 2 for the semi-truck was changed with 
-10 mm. In section 2 the semi-truck’s offset was changed with 20 mm for pass 3. The dump 
truck’s offset was changed with -40 mm for pass 1, and -20 mm for pass 3. For section 3 the 
change was done for the semi-truck with a change of 10 mm for pass 2. 
 
==PAGE:96==
96 
 
5.3.5 Correlation coefficient and modern agreement metric 
The Pearson product-moment correlation coefficient and modern agreement metric (Levenberg 
et al. 2018) are calculated to give an objective evaluation of the validation effort. The Pearson 
product-moment correlation coefficient r describes the linear relationship strength and 
direction between two data sets, and ranges between -1 and 1. r is calculated by (Levenberg et 
al. 2018): 
𝑟=
∑
(𝑋𝑛−𝑋̅)(𝑌𝑛−𝑌̅)
𝑁
𝑛=1
𝑁𝜎𝑋𝜎𝑌
 
(56) 
Where 𝑋𝑛 and 𝑌𝑛 are the values in the correlated data sets, N is the number of values in the data 
sets, 𝑋̅ and 𝑌̅ are the respective means of 𝑋𝑛 and 𝑌𝑛, and 𝜎𝑋 and 𝜎𝑌 are the corresponding 
standard deviations. A verbal description corresponding to correlation ranges can be seen in 
Table 35. Because r is scale-blind the modern agreement metric 𝜆 is also calculated. For 
positive r values, 𝜆 can be calculated by (Levenberg et al. 2018): 
𝜆=
2𝑟
𝜎𝑋
𝜎𝑌+ 𝜎𝑌
𝜎𝑋+ (𝑋̅ −𝑌̅)2
𝜎𝑋𝜎𝑌
,
(𝑟≥0) 
(57) 
𝜆 ranges from 0 to 1, and considers both correlation and bias, and is symmetric and 
dimensionless. 
Table 35 Corresponding verbal description of correlation ranges. Table originally from Levenberg et al. (2018). 
 
 
5.3.6 Slopes of strain data 
For most of the strain responses, measured by the transverse sensors, a noticeable sensor drift 
occurred. To mitigate the effect of the drift when calculating the correlation coefficient and the 
modern agreement metric, the slope was taken of both the measured and calculated strains. 
Both coefficients where then also calculated for the slopes of the data. This was only done for 
the transverse strain responses since it was these with a pronounced sensor drift. The slopes for 
the data were calculated between two points as (𝜀′ ≈Δ𝜀/Δ𝑡) for all the data points. 
 
 
==PAGE:97==
97 
 
5.3.7 Frequency filter 
The strain measurements were filtered by using a “near-perfect” low-pass filter made according 
to Nielsen (2019).  
The filter has been used to remove some of the higher frequencies that were considered noise. 
This was especially necessary for the measurements of sensor 3 in section 1 and section 2 for 
the IFV since these measurements were practically unreadable. Because the slope is very 
sensitive to noise, it was also necessary to filter the data to find the slope. 
Over filtering the data, meaning choosing a too low cutting frequency, can cause the responses 
to seem smaller than they actually are. Over filtering can also cause multiple peaks to “melt” 
together. Because of these reasons it is important not to over filter the data. 
For the IFV, the rollers are much closer together than the axels are on the other vehicles. This 
means the loading frequency is higher for the IFV. Other than that, the IFV also caused some 
vibrations with even higher frequencies. These two things made it much more difficult to not 
over filter the data. 
The vibrations cause a relatively small change in the response, and they made it very 
impractical to compare the slope of the measured data with that of the calculated response. 
Because of this, the frequencies corresponding to the vibrations have been almost entirely 
removed from the data of the transverse sensors. There was not a big problem with sensor drift 
for the longitudinal sensors, so the slope was not needed, and therefore the vibrations were not 
removed. Examples of strain curves before and after filtering can be seen in Figure 93, Figure 
94 and Figure 95. The IFV responses have been chosen for these examples because they were 
the most difficult to filter. 
Figure 93 shows a case where the data might have been a little over filtered due to the much 
larger than normal amount of noise. The problem is that it can be difficult to see how large the 
response is supposed to be without the noise. Due to the large noise, this is most likely the 
worst case of over filtering.

frequency corresponding to the noise and the frequency corresponding to the vibrations have 
been removed. It is seen that this is most relevant for the two outer troughs.

5.3.8 Validation plots 
5.3.8.1 IFV 
Since it is assumed in the code that the contact patches are rectangular, the patch for the IFV 
has been simplified to a rectangle with length 100 mm and width of 220 mm. The patch is filled 
with micro circles with a radius of 𝑎=10 mm. The pitch distance between patches is 155 mm. 
During the geometric measurement of the IFV, it was found that the placement of the rollers 
was different for the two sides of the vehicle and that the number of contact patches at a given 
time was different as well. For the input, the two tracks of the IFV are assumed to be identical 
with 2470 mm between them. The distance between rollers has been rounded to nearest half 
pitch, meaning that if one roller is directly above one patch at a given time the other rollers will 
be either directly above one patch or in the middle between two patches.     
When weighing the IFV, the weight was found for each roller. However, to model the 
movement, the load distribution over time is needed for a patch. This distribution has not been 
measured and therefore must be assumed based on the weights of the rollers. It is assumed that 
when a roller is directly over a patch, the entire load of that roller will be distributed through 
that patch alone, and at any other time the load will be distributed between two patches. The 
load is assumed to vary linearly from one patch to the next. 
This assumption is the reason why the calculated strains also vary very linearly between points. 
This would change with a more realistic load history. However, the linear variation seems to 
give a decent model to compare with the measurements. 
Since the offset between the sensor and contact patch in the x-direction is unknown, multiple 
calculations have been made. It is assumed that the maximum response will occur if the patch 
is directly on top of the sensor, and the smallest possible response is when the sensor is in the 
middle between two patches. These are therefore both calculated to estimate an upper (x-
offset1) and lower (x-offset2) bound. If the measured response lies in between or close to the 
calculations the fit is considered good. 
5.3.8.1.1 Section 0 
An example of measured data compared with calculated data for the IFV can be seen on Figure 
96 to Figure 101. The measured data has been filtered as described before. With this the 
correlation coefficient r and modern agreement metric 𝜆 are calculated and seen in Table 36 
and Table 37. The fit of the data is also illustrated in cross-plots, Figure 102 and in Figure 103 
for the slopes of the data. 
For Figure 96, the validation plot for sensor 1, the calculated data follows the trend of the 
measured data. The response has seven troughs representing the load from the rollers. The 
difference between the responses is mostly small, especially with this low of a strain 
measurement, where the sensors precision could be put in question but there are some 
differences in the response. This could also stem from the uncertainties and assumptions for 
inputs for the code such as the driving offset, sensor placement, base layer modulus estimation, 
and the filtering of the data. The mechanistic code would also be expected to have some error 
from the numerical calculations, and these would have a higher significance for lower strain 
calculations. 
 
==PAGE:100==
100 
 
 
The correlation coefficient r and modern agreement metric 𝜆 are both okay (0.63<), seen in 
Table 36 and Table 37. And the correlation coefficient can be deemed “strong”, from Table 35, 
which may indicate a fine fit. The λ is not the best, but this would be expected to be because of 
the reasons stated above and the unwanted data/noise that was not removed. The cross-plots 
from Figure 102 shows the measured response and calculated response has fine 
correspondence.

An example of measured data compared with calculated data for the IFV can be seen on Figure

correlation coefficient r and modern agreement metric 𝜆 are calculated, seen in Table 38 and

measured data. The response has seven troughs representing the load from the rollers. The 
difference between the responses is small but there are some differences. This could because 
of it is a low strain measurement, where the sensors measurement precision could be put into 
question. Also, the difference in trough magnitude could stem from the different uncertainties 
and assumptions as stated for section 0, such as the driving offset, sensor placement, and 
estimated base layer modulus. The vibrations from the IFV could also lead to the less desirable 
fit. And of course, this could also be because of effects the linear elastic model can not predict. 
The correlation coefficient r and modern agreement metric 𝜆 both are okay (0.59<), seen in 
Table 38 and Table 39. But with the aforementioned reasons, it would be expected not to be 
the best. And the correlation coefficient can be deemed “strong”, from Table 35, indicating a 
fine fit. The cross-plots from Figure 109 shows the measured response and calculated response 
has fine correspondence.

5.3.8.1.3 Section 2 
In the following the measured strains in section 2 for the first pass of the IFV will be compared 
to the calculated responses for the code. The measured data has been filtered to remove some 
of the higher frequencies. This was done to reduce the noise seen in the raw data in Chapter 
4.3.5.  
Figure 111 shows the measurements for sensor 2 together with the calculated strains. Both the 
measured strain and the calculated strain show the seven troughs corresponding to the seven 
rollers of the IFV.  
The peak values are approximately the same for all three plots however the troughs are varying 
a bit in size. For most of the troughs x-offset 1 overestimates the strain by a lot where x-offset 
2 fits better.  This is also supported by the 𝜆 value for x-offset 2 being a little better than for x-
offset 1, both can be seen in Table 41.  
The r values can be seen in Table 40. They are close to the same value for the two offsets. 
Following Table 35 the correlation is strong for x-offset 1 and very strong for x-offset 2. 
==PAGE:110==
110 
 
 
 
Figure 111 Validation plot for the IFV for section 2 pass 1 sensor 2. 
 
Figure 112 shows the measurements for sensor 3 together with the calculated strains. Both the 
measured strain and the calculated strain show the seven troughs corresponding to the seven 
rollers of the IFV.  
For Figure 112 the calculated strains for the two offsets are much closer to each other than for 
Figure 111. This is due to sensor 3 being further from the load than sensor 2. The 𝜆 values in 
Table 41 also indicates they fit just about the same to the measured data. Some of the troughs 
and peaks fits better than others but the overall magnitude seems to be not that far off.  
It should be noted that the raw data for this sensor had severe problems with noise to a degree 
where the response was practically unrecognizable. This means that it was difficult to decide a 
cut off frequency and the data may very well be a little over filtered. This might be a big part 
of the reason for some of the peaks and troughs having a good fit while others have a bad fit. 
Table 40 shows that the correlation for both of the calculated graphs are in the high end of 
strong.  
==PAGE:111==
111 
 
 
Figure 112 Validation plot for the IFV for section 2 pass 1 sensor 3. 
Figure 113 shows the measurements for sensor 4 together with the calculated strains. Both the 
measured strain and the calculated strain show the seven peaks corresponding to the seven 
rollers of the IFV.  
The two calculated responses are very similar in size which is very common for the transverse 
measurements. In Table 41 the 𝜆 values can be seen to be close to the same value which is way 
lower than those for the longitudinal measurements. The lower 𝜆 values is largely due to the 
sensor drift. First the values seem to drift a little into the negative values and then into the 
positive. 
Considering only the amplitudes of the peaks, the calculated data seems to overestimate the 
strains a bit, however the strains are relatively small, so the difference is not more than a couple 
of micro strains. 
Table 40 shows that the correlation is not as good as it was for the longitudinal sensors when 
comparing the strains, but when comparing the slopes instead the correlation is just as good. 
According to Table 35 the correlation is moderate for Figure 113 but in the boundary between 
strong and very strong when comparing the slopes. The 𝜆 values for the slope in Table 41 are 
also much better. 
==PAGE:112==

Figure 113 Validation plot for the IFV for section 2 pass 1 sensor 4. 
Figure 114 shows the measurements for sensor 5 together with the calculated strains. Both the 
measured strain and the calculated strain show the seven troughs corresponding to the seven 
rollers of the IFV.  However, the two outer peaks are barely visibly on the measured data. This 
was also the case for the raw data in Chapter 4.3.5 meaning this is not due to over filtering, but 
just how the measured data looks. The first trough for the calculated graphs also looks like it 
is about to disappear, however the last trough is still clearly visible. 
The two calculated responses are relatively similar in size which is very common for the 
transverse measurements. In Table 41 the 𝜆 values can be seen to be the same. They are lower 
than those for the longitudinal measurements, but this is largely due to the sensor drift. Sensor 
5 has an especially large sensor drift compared to the other transverse sensors for pass 1. 
When only considering the magnitude of the peaks, those from the calculated responses seem 
to be a little lager than those from the measured. However, the absolute strain is still much 
larger for the measurement, and it is difficult to tell exactly how much of this is due to sensor 
drift. 
Table 40 shows that the correlation between the calculated and measured responses is actually 
very strong according to Table 35.  
The data from sensor 5 in this pass seemed to be more sensitive to the frequency filter. This 
means that the cut off frequency for this was much higher than for most other passes and 
sensors. Because of this the correlation of the slope is not as good as for the other transverse 
sensors due to the slope being very sensitive to noise.

assumptions and simplifications into account the calculations were never expected to be 
flawless. For example, all layers are assumed linear elastic in the model, when in reality the 
base layer is not linear, and the asphalt layer is closer to viscoelastic. 
Taking assumptions and simplifications into account, the responses from computational model 
fits very well to the measured responses in general.

5.3.8.1.4 Section 3 
The validation plots of the individual sensors for the IFV in section 3 can be seen on Figure 
118 to Figure 123. Table 42 shows the corresponding Pearson product-moment correlation 
coefficients r and Table 43 shows the modern agreement metrics 𝜆 for all the sensors. Table 
42 and Table 43 also show the coefficients for the slopes for the transverse sensors. This is 
done to mitigate the influence of sensor drift. The cross-plots for all the sensors are shown in 
Figure 124 while the cross-plots for the slopes for the transverse sensors are shown in Figure 
125.  
Figure 118 shows the validation plot for sensor 1. There are seven troughs corresponding to 
the seven rollers on the IFV. For troughs number 1 and 7 the interval of the modelled pavement 
responses are smaller than the measured, while troughs 2 and 5 are larger and the rest align 
with the measured pavement responses. Both r and 𝜆  are shown in Table 50 and Table 51. The 
correlation falls in the category strong following Table 35, while the modern agreement metric 
==PAGE:116==
116 
 
is decent. The cross-plot for sensor 1 in Figure 124 also shows a good correspondence between 
the measured and modelled pavement response. A reason for the modern agreement metric not 
being higher could stem from the fact that the considered strains are small. When they are small 
other factors, like vibrations, play a larger role in the shape and size of the response. For the 
IFV larger vibrations could be felt than for the other vehicles, so there is a bigger likelihood of 
them having an effect on the response.  
 
Figure 118 Validation plot for the IFV for section 3 pass 1 sensor 1. 
Figure 119 shows the validation plot for sensor 2. There are seven troughs corresponding to 
the seven rollers on the IFV. For troughs number 2, 4, 5, and 6 the interval of the modelled 
pavement responses are larger than the measured, while troughs 1, 3, and 7 approximately 
aligns with the measured pavement responses. The differences between the troughs seem to be 
within reason. This is supported by r and 𝜆, shown in Table 50 and Table 51, being high. The 
correlation falls in the category very strong following Table 35. The cross-plot for sensor 2 in 
Figure 124 also shows the correspondence between the measured and modelled pavement 
response.

5.3.8.2 Dump truck 
In the computational model it was assumed that all contact patches are rectangular. Therefore, 
the contact area between the asphalt and the dump truck has been simplified.  
The dump truck has two “rows” of ribs per tire, and therefore a rib from each has been 
modelled. Both ribs have been split into three parts to make it possible to vary the load, making 
a total of six patches. The load distribution has not been measured, but after some iterations a 
suitable match was found to be 25% of the load from the tire being distributed on each of the 
inner patches, both with length 100 mm and width 80 mm, and the other 50% load being 
distributed evenly between the other four patches all with length 140 mm and width 80 mm. 
The patches are filled with micro circles of radius 𝑎=10 mm. The patches have been rotated 
with an angel of either  𝜃1 = 𝜋/3 or 𝜃2 = −𝜋/3.  
 
Figure 126 Modelling of the dump truck patch configuration 
When weighing the dump truck, the weight was found for each tire. However, to model the 
movement, the load distribution over time for a single patch is needed. This distribution has 
not been measured and therefore had to be assumed based on the weights of the tires.  
It is assumed that the load of a tire is distributed evenly between the two modelled patches 
when the axel is directly in between them. The additional assumptions for the dump truck are: 
• Loads vary linearly between patches in the same “row”. 
• Max two patch configurations (12 patches in total) are active at the same time for each 
tire.  
• Contact patches are identical for each tire. 
• Patches from different tires in the same side of the vehicle follows the same “row”.  
The assumption that the load varies linearly is the reason why the calculated strains also vary 
linearly between points. This should change with a more realistic load history. However, the 
linear variation is found to give both a suitable overall form and peak/trough values of the 
graphs.  
Since the offset between the sensor and contact patch in the x-direction is unknown, multiple 
calculations has been made for the dump truck. 
It is not clear which offset will a maximum and minimum response history. Therefore, the 
response is calculated for four different offsets. This will not necessarily give the maximum 
and minimum response history, but it is assumed to give a decent estimation of the interval.  
==PAGE:122==
122 
 
x-offset 1 is zero offset, x-offset 2 is ¼ pitch offset, x-offset 3 is ½ pitch offset, and x-offset 3 
is ¾ pitch offset. If the measured response lies in between or close to the calculations the fit is 
considered good. 
The distance between tires on the same axel are set to 2440 mm. The pitch distance between 
patches used are 205 mm. 
For the dump truck the offsets presented in Table 14 are to the line D8 (see Figure 10), and not 
to a defined point on the modelled patch. To take this into account 40 mm are added to the 
offsets for all passes for the dump truck. The 40 mm stems from geometric considerations 
regarding the inner patches and the actual patches on the dump truck’s wheels. 
 
5.3.8.2.1 Section 0  
The validation plots of the individual sensors for the dump truck in section 0 can be seen on 
Figure 127 to Figure 132. Table 44 shows the corresponding Pearson product-moment 
correlation coefficients r and Table 45 shows the modern agreement metrics 𝜆 for all the 
sensors. Table 44 and Table 45 also show the coefficients for the slopes for the transverse 
sensors. This is done to mitigate the influence of sensor drift. The cross-plots for all the sensors 
are shown in Figure 133 while the cross-plots for the slopes for the transverse sensors are shown 
in Figure 134.  
Figure 127 shows the validation plot for sensor 1. There are three troughs corresponding to the 
three axles on the dump truck. All three troughs for the interval of the modelled pavement 
responses approximately align with the measured. Both r and 𝜆 shown in Table 44 and Table 
45 are high. The correlation falls in the category very strong following Table 35. The cross-
plot for sensor 1 in Figure 133 also shows a good correspondence between the measured and 
modelled pavement response.

An example of measured data compared with calculated data for the dump truck can be seen 
on Figure 135 to Figure 139. The measured data has been filtered as described before. With 
this the correlation coefficient r and modern agreement metric 𝜆 is calculated, seen in Table 46 
and Table 47. The fit of the data is also illustrated in cross-plots, Figure 140 and Figure 141. 
For Figure 135, the validation plot for sensor 1, the calculated data follows the trend of the 
measured data. The response has three troughs representing the load from the axles. The 
difference between the calculated and measured response is small and within what would be 
expected. The correlation coefficient r and modern agreement metric λ both are high (0.87<), 
seen in Table 46 and Table 47, and the correlation coefficient can be deemed “very strong”, 
from Table 35, establishing the fit is good. The cross-plots from Figure 140 shows the measured 
response and calculated response has good correspondence.

5.3.8.2.3 Section 2 
In the following the measured strains in section 2 for the first pass of the dump truck will be 
compared to the calculated responses from the code. The measured data has been filtered to 
remove some of the higher frequencies. This was done to reduce the noise seen in the raw data 
in Chapter 4.3.5. When modeling this pass it was necessary to add an additional 40 mm to the 
measured offset to get a good fit. This can be caused by the estimation of the patch geometry 
or an error in the measured distance. 
In Figure 142 the measured strain for sensor 2 is shown together with the calculated strain from 
the code. There are three troughs corresponding to each of the axels on the dump truck passing 
the sensor.  
The measurement lies in between some of the calculated graphs which is very good. The overall 
form also seems to be a decent fit. There are some minor problems shortly after each of the 
troughs, and the last trough of the calculation is a little displaced to the right. The displacement 
of the last trough could be caused by a change in velocity during the test. However, because 
this seems to be a reoccurring problem, it is more likely caused by a small error in the modeled 
distance between the axels. In Table 48 and Table 49 it is seen that the correlation between all 
calculated graphs and the measurements are very strong and that 𝜆 is also very good. 
 
Figure 142 Validation plot for the dump truck for section 2 pass 1 sensor 2. 
==PAGE:133==
133 
 
In Figure 143 the measured strain for sensor 3 is shown together with the calculated strain from 
the code. There are three troughs corresponding to each of the axels on the dump truck passing 
the sensor.  
The calculated graphs do not differ from each other as much as they did for sensor 2. This is 
because sensor 2 was closer to the middle of the tire. 
The calculated graphs seem to fit well to the measured data overall. There are some minor 
problems shortly before and after some of the troughs, and the last trough of the calculation is 
displaced a little to the right. The displacement of the last trough could be caused by a change 
in velocity during the test. However, because this seems to be a reoccurring problem, it is more 
likely to be caused by a small error in the model distance between the axels. 
In Table 48 and Table 49 it is seen that the correlation between all calculated graphs and the 
measurements are “very strong” and that 𝜆 is also very good. 
 
 
Figure 143 Validation plot for the dump truck for section 2 pass 1 sensor 3.

the code. There are three peaks corresponding to each of the axels on the dump truck passing 
the sensor.  
The calculated responses are very similar in size which is very common for the transverse 
measurements. The calculated strains seem to be smaller in general but a lot of this seems to 
be caused by some sensor drift.  
In Table 48 and Table 49 it is seen that the correlation between all calculated graphs and the 
measurements are “strong” but 𝜆 is not so good. The correlation between the slope of the 
calculated graphs and the slope of the measurements are “very strong” and 𝜆 is very good 
indicating that a lot of the difference in Figure 144 is caused by the sensor drift.

5.3.8.3 Semi-truck 
When modeling the semi-truck in the code, each of the three tires in one side of the vehicle is 
treated as a patch. The size of these patches is chosen based on the measured pressure length 
and width of the tires. The length of all tires is set to 220 mm, the width of the front tire is set 
to 340 mm, and the width of the back tires are set to 280 mm. The patches are filled with micro 
circles of radius 𝑎=10 mm. 
The code is made for vehicles that periodically lifts and places ribs on the ground. This is not 
the case for the semi-truck. Therefore, the pitch distance is kept very short, and the load is 
transferred from one patch to the next in one step. Doing this corresponds to modeling the 
vehicle as sliding on the pavement. Since the code was not made for modeling sliding it is not 
very efficient at it and the calculations might take a bit longer. The pitch was set to 10 mm 
because it seemed to give a decent representation of sliding without using too much 
computational time or memory to run. The best way to see that the chosen pitch is sufficient is 
that the plot for the calculations seem smooth. 
The front axle has 37.6% of the total load, and the rear axle has 62.4% which is evenly 
distributed between its tires. 
Since the semi-truck does not periodically lift and lower ribs, an offset in the x-direction should 
not matter. Therefore, only one calculation is made, and if the measured response lies close to 
the calculation the fit is considered good. 
5.3.8.3.1 Section 0  
An example of measured data compared with calculated data for the semi-truck can be seen on 
Figure 157 to Figure 162. The measured data has been filtered as described before. With this 
the correlation coefficient r and modern agreement metric 𝜆 is calculated, seen in Table 52. 
The fit of the data is also illustrated in cross-plots, Figure 163 and Figure 164.  
For Figure 157, the validation plot for sensor 1, the calculated data follows the trend of the 
measured data. The response has two troughs representing the load from the axles. The 
difference between the responses is small and within what could be expected for this linear 
elastic model with all the assumptions stated. When finding the base layer modulus for section 
0, it is assumed it should be the same as for section 1. But because of this the calculated 
response is not as good as possible. This could be because of different code input uncertainties 
==PAGE:143==

measurement and sensor placement not being precise can also be a part of the problem. 
The correlation coefficient r and the modern agreement metric 𝜆 are both high (0.86<), seen in 
Table 52, and the correlation coefficient can be deemed “very strong”, from Table 35, implying 
the fit is good. The cross-plots from Figure 163 shows the measured response and calculated 
response has a good correspondence.

5.4 Effect of grid reinforcement implementation 
The effect of implementing the reinforcement grid in the input for the computational model has 
been investigated by using the material parameters of section 0 and adding a reinforcement 
grid in various heights and calculating some of the responses. 
In Figure 187 to Figure 190, calculations have been made for four cases. The first case is with 
no reinforcement grid at all, this is plotted with a blue line. The second case, with a grid placed 
in the bottom of the asphalt layer, is plotted with an orange line. Case three is with a grid 
imbedded 110 mm into the asphalt and is plotted with a yellow line. The last case where the 
grid is embedded 40 mm into the asphalt is shown with a purple line. All four cases have been 
calculated as the semi-truck driving with 10 km/h with the middle of the front tire driving over 
the evaluation point. 
Figure 187 shows the strain in the x-direction. It is clearly seen that the grid in the bottom of 
the asphalt layer is the one that reduces the strain in the bottom of the asphalt layer the most.  
 
 
Figure 187 Horizontal strain in the x-direction at the bottom of the asphalt layer. 
 
 
==PAGE:162==
162 
 
Figure 188 shows the strain in the y-direction. It is clearly seen that the grid in the bottom of 
the asphalt layer is the one that reduces the strain in the bottom of the asphalt layer the most.  
 
Figure 188 Horizontal strain in the y-direction at the bottom of the asphalt layer. 
 
Figure 189 shows the vertical stress in the top of the base layer. It seems that the grid in the 
bottom of the asphalt layer also helps a little bit better than the other two but not by a lot. It 
seems like having a grid helps a bit, but the placement of the grid is not of great importance. 
 
Figure 189 Vertical stress at the top of the base layer 
==PAGE:163==
163 
 
Figure 190 shows the vertical stress in the top of the subgrade. In this depth the effect of the 
grid seems to be negligible. The only graph that stands out just a little is the one with the grid 
being modeled 40 mm into the asphalt. 
 
Figure 190 Vertical stress at the top of the subgrade layer. 
 
Form Figure 187 to Figure 190 it is found that according to the computational model, the best 
placement for the reinforcement grid to reduce the strains and stresses, is in the bottom of the 
asphalt layer.  In general, it seems that placing the grid 40 mm into the asphalt does very little 
to no difference.  The grid imbedded 110 mm into the asphalt has a positive influence on strains 
but less than the grid at the bottom. 
In Figure 191 to Figure 193 the effect of implementing the grid in the model has been further 
investigated. For the semi-truck, the highest strain measurements in each of the sections with 
grid-reinforcement from the validation (see Chapter 5.3.8), has been plotted together with the 
original calculations with the grid, and an additional calculation without a grid. 
Figure 191 shows the measured and calculated strain for sensor 3 in section 1 for pass 2 with 
the semi-truck. For this section the implementation of the grid makes a clear difference.  Now 
considering only the highest absolute value, the calculation with a grid is about 17.7% smaller 
than the measured and the one without a grid is 0.4% larger. 
==PAGE:164==
164 
 
 
Figure 191 Strain measurement and calculation with and without grid reinforcement for the semi-truck in section 1 pass 2 
for sensor 3. 
 
Figure 192 shows the measured and calculated strain for sensor 3 in section 2 for pass 2 with 
the semi-truck. For this section the implementation of the grid does not make as much 
difference as for section 1. If considering only the highest absolute value, the calculation with 
a grid is about 8.3% smaller than the measured and the one without a grid is 3.4% smaller. 
 
 
Figure 192 Strain measurement and calculation with and without grid reinforcement for the semi-truck in section 2 pass 2 
for sensor 3. 
 
 
==PAGE:165==
165 
 
Figure 193 shows the measured and calculated strain for sensor 3 in section 3 for pass 2 with 
the semi-truck. Just as for section 2 the implementation of the grid does not make as much 
difference as in section 1. If considering only the highest absolute value, the calculation with a 
grid is about 6.3% smaller than the measured and the one without a grid is 2.6% smaller.  
 
 
Figure 193 Strain measurement and calculation with and without grid reinforcement for the semi-truck in section 3 pass 2 
for sensor 3. 
 
For sensor 3 in all three sections shown in Figure 191 and Figure 193, the model without grid 
seems to fit a little better. This can be due to the elastic modulus or sensor displacement being 
a bit wrong, or simply due to some of the simplifications in the model. For example, it is 
assumed that there is full bonding between layers which might not be true in the case with a 
grid. From Figure 187 to Figure 193 it seems that the only section where it makes a big 
difference to implement the grid is section 1. In section 2 and 3 modeling the grid makes such 
a little difference that other uncertainties might have a bigger influence. 
 
5.5 Summary 
Main findings from the verification showed the tendency of smaller loading circles and thereby 
more loading circles, lead to lower errors when comparing to analytical solutions. 
It was also found from the verification, with the analytical solutions, that there was a point 
where the amount of loading circles would not change the precision of the calculations in an 
amount that would not be worth it for the extra computational time. This would be expected to 
be around 100-400 circles, but even the lowest amount tested for, 4 circles, gives a relatively 
good result of with the highest error of 7.8 %. 
Comparing the half-space simulation from the code, to another program ELLEA1, showed 
results that were close, which resulted in low errors with an outlier of 4% and all others under 
1%. There was also seen a bigger difference close to the top of the surface and at large depths, 
but again the difference is low. 
==PAGE:166==
166 
 
For the other cases where x and y have different values, the general error is small (<1%), 
meaning that the difference between the code and ELLEA1 is small. There are seen some parts 
with higher errors, >~10%, compared to the others. This was mostly for small absolute 
microstrain values (1>|𝜇𝜀|). 
From the validation of the mechanistic code, it was found that the code generally can predict 
the response of the sensers well, with the assumed pavement parameters. But there are some 
reservations from the assumptions behind some of the input parameters that are used to predict 
the response. The driving offset from the sensors is estimated because the exact sensor 
placement is unknown to a degree, and because of uncertainties in the measured driving offset. 
Therefore, the assumed placement can be wrong. The base layer modulus is also estimated. 
This is because of the FWD and backcalculation could not predict a reasonable result because 
of the thickness of the asphalt, the low temperature, and geophone placements, resulting in the 
asphalt layer reducing the base layer modulus (see Chapter 4.2.2). The base layer could also 
change its modulus depending on the loading. For the sensors there were observed a large 
amount of sensor drift resulting in difficulty in comparing the calculations to the measured 
data, since this can make the measured response appearance incorrect. There are also observed 
unwanted load responses or noise in the data that makes it more difficult to predict the response 
and therefor filtering was done, but even this could not remove it all. And of course the 
simplification by using a linear elastic theory to predict the response can not result in a perfect 
fit. Even with all this, the parameters used to show the fit of the data, the correlation coefficient 
and modern agreement metric, shows generally high values and therefore showing the 
predictions are good. 
 
 
==PAGE:167==
167 
 
6 Pavement analysis 
6.1 Pavement systems 
The responses from the off-road vehicles, semi-truck and an Equivalent Single Axle Load 
(ESAL) are compared for four different synthetic pavement systems. The synthetic pavement 
systems are based on traffic class T2 to T5 from catalogue road pavements (Vejregler 2022), 
with an illustration of their compositions in Figure 194. The used Poisson’s ratio 𝜈= 0.35 was 
the same for all layers in all 4 systems. The responses are generated for two different driving 
speeds 𝑉1 = 5 km/h and 𝑉2 = 50 km/h. 𝑉1 was chosen to be a slow driving speed, while 𝑉2 
was chosen to be a faster driving speed. 50 km/h was chosen since this is the maximum driving 
speed for the dump truck (Hydrema 922D). The driving speeds are included in the calculations 
by using the speed correction factor (52) described in section 5.3.2. 
 
The abbreviations used in Figure 194 are: 
AC is asphalt concrete 
GAC is hot mix gravel (asphalt concrete) 
BL is the base layer made of stabilized gravel 
SB is the subbase course, sand 
SG is the subgrade (frost susceptible) 
 
 
 
Figure 194 Illustration of synthetic pavement systems made after/based on traffic classes. 
 
 
==PAGE:168==
168 
 
6.2 ESAL definition 
An Equivalent Single Axle Load (ESAL) is the standard axle that is used when dimensioning 
a road. The maximum allowable axle weight in Denmark is 10 tons (exceptions do exist), and 
the maximum allowable width is 2.55 m (again exceptions do exist) (Transportministeriet 
2016). The Danish definition for the ESAL is an equivalent 10-ton axle with dual mounted 
tires, a tire pressure of 𝜎0 = 0.7 MPa, and a distance between the middle of the dual tires (same 
side of the axle) of 350 mm (Vejregler 2022). This definition leads to all the wheels carrying a 
load of: 
𝑃= 1
4 ⋅10,000 kg ⋅9.82 m
s2 = 24.55 ⋅103 N 
(58) 
Where the gravitational constant 𝑔= 9.82 
m
s2 was used, since it is the Danish gravitational 
constant. 
When modelling the ESAL the contact pressure is modelled as a circular load with the imprint 
radius a found by the formula: 
𝑎= √
𝑃
𝜎0 ⋅π = √24.55 ⋅103 N
0.7 MPa ⋅π = 106 mm 
(59) 
No specification of the distance between the two outer most wheels (on different sides of the 
axle) was found, therefore the distance was chosen to be 2.4 m (between the outer sides of the 
outer most wheels). The choice based on the distance being a little shorter than the maximum 
allowable width (2.55 m). 
 
6.3 Comparison between vehicles 
When dimensioning a pavement according to Vejregler (2022), the criteria concern the 
horizontal tensile strains at the underside of the asphalt layer, and the vertical compressive 
stress at the upper side of the unbound layers. These locations are therefore compared between 
the different vehicles. The maximum octahedral shear stress in the asphalt layer is also 
compared because of rutting (Wang 2009). When looking at the underside of the asphalt layer, 
0.01 mm was subtracted from the depth (AC thickness), to ensure the value was found in the 
AC layer and not in the base layer. The same was done when looking at the upper side of a 
layer, except 0.01 mm was added instead of subtracted from the depth. 
In the comparisons the maximum values from a vehicle driving along the x-axis at a y-offset 
are compared. The z-values are dependent on what is compared. 
 
 
==PAGE:169==
169 
 
6.3.1 Search locations 
When modelling the vehicles driving along the x-axis, there will be a difference in the response 
size dependant on where the patches land compared to the evaluation point. To take this into 
account an x-offset (can be between zero and a pitch distance) is chosen.  
When the x-offset and y-offset is 0 for the IFV the evaluation point lies under the middle of the 
patch. When they are 0 for the semi-truck and ESAL the evaluation point lies under the middle 
of the outer rear wheel (outer wheel for the ESAL). When the offset is 0 for the dump truck the 
evaluation point lies under the middle innermost rectangle making up the patch (see Figure 
126). The patch is the one closest to the middle of the dump truck on the right wheel. The x-
offset describes the distance from the 0 point (described above) to the patch. 
For the IFV the patches are modelled as (single) rectangles which causes it to be double 
symmetric. Because of this the maximum values will be found at the middle of the patch (x=0, 
y=0). For the dump truck the maximum values are sought under the width of a patch (in the y-
direction) and along a pitch (in the x-direction). Since the patches on the dump truck are the 
same just mirrored along the middle of the wheel it is only necessary to examine under one of 
them. For the semi-truck and the ESAL the maximum values are sought under the outer side 
of the outer rear wheel to the middle between the two rear wheels. A step size of 20 mm is used 
in both the x- and y-direction in the searches. A smaller step size was not chosen because of 
considerations regarding computational times. 
For octahedral shear stresses the same search areas and step sizes are used in the x- and y-
directions as for the other maximum values. The asphalt layers are furthermore divided with a 
step size of 10 mm in the z-direction. The underside of the asphalt is also examined. This is 
done for all the traffic classes and for both driving speeds considered.

Figure 204 Maximum octahedral shear stress in the AC layer for V=50 km/h. The ESAL corresponds to 100%. 
 
6.3.4 Main takeaways 
6.3.4.1 Semi-truck 
For the horizontal tensile strains at the underside of the AC, the semi-truck results in about the 
same size of strains as the ESAL, for both speeds, 5 km/h and 50 km/h. For the vertical 
compressive stress at the upper side of the base layer the semi-truck results in lower stresses 
than the ESAL for both speeds. For the semi-truck the vertical compressive stresses at the upper 
side of the subbase layer are higher than for the ESAL for both speeds. For the vertical 
compressive stress at the upper side of the subgrade layer, the ratios between the stresses caused 
by the semi-truck and the ESAL, remain approximately the same for the two speeds, with the 
semi-truck resulting in higher stresses. For the semi-truck the maximum octahedral shear 
stresses are lower than for the ESAL. The maximum octahedral shear stress occurred at the 
underside of the AC for all traffic classes at both speeds. The percentages for the semi-truck, 
when the ESAL corresponds to 100%, generally rise with an increase in the traffic class. All 
the responses from the semi-truck remain within an interval of ±15% when comparing to the 
ESAL responses.

Octahedral shear stress in the AC τoct V=50km/h

==PAGE:178==
